Ninth and Tenth Refutations: Existence and Substance Dependence
Ninth and tenth refutations on existence and substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the final two refutations: that existence is one attribute but the servant can acquire it only for some quiddities, and that a substance depends on the agent at origination but not during persistence.

Core Idea
Even when things share the same quiddity or attribute, their dependence on the agent can differ over time.

The author wraps up with two final examples. The ninth returns to the nature of existence and human capability; the tenth discusses how a substance's need for an agent changes from origination to persistence.

Guided Path
1
Read the ninth and tenth examples
Read the Arabic and translation for the final two counterexamples.
2
Ninth example: existence and human capability
Existence is one attribute, but the servant can only acquire existence for some quiddities, not all.
3
Tenth example: substance dependence
A substance needs an agent at origination but not during persistence, showing that a single essence can have different rulings over time.
4
Draw the overall conclusion
The Mu'tazilite principle that like things have like rulings is definitively refuted by these ten examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك التعين شرطاً لحصول ذلك الحكم، في أحد الطرفين، أو مانعاً منه في الطرف الآخر؟. سلمنا: أن التعين ساقط الاعتبار، لكن قولكم: ويصح على الشيء ما يصح على مثله، منقوض بصور كثيرة: الأول: إن أفراد النوع الأخير متساوية في تمام الماهية. مع أن تعين هذا يمنع حصوله لذاك، وتعين ذاك يمتنع حصوله لهذا. وإلا لصار هذا عين ذاك، وذاك عين هذا. وكل ذلك محال فمحال أن يكون مقدور العبد مثل المقدور الله تعالى. ثم لم يلزم منه أن يكون مقدور العبد بحيث أن يكون مقدوراً لله تعالى - على مذهب المعتزلة - لأن عندهم مقدور واحد بين قادرين محال. الثالث: إن مشايخ المعتزلة: مذهبهم: أن الذوات متساوية في كونها ذوات. وإنما يخالف بعضها بعضاً لأجل اختصاص كل واحد منها بصفته الخاصة. فعلى هذا: ذات الله تعالى مساوية لسائر الذوات في الذاتية. ثم لم يلزم منه أن يصح على كل واحد من الذوات كل ما يصح على سائر الذوات. وإلا لزم أن يصح على ذات الله: الحدوث والإمكان، والحاجة إلى المحل، وأن يصح على ذوات المحدثات كونها قديمة، واجبة الوجود. وكل ذلك محال. الرابع: إن صفة الوجود - عندهم - صفة واحدة. فيكون الوجود الحاصل في السواد والبياض: مساوياً للوجود الحاصل في ذات الله. ولم يلزم الاستواء في جميع الأحكام. الخامس: عندهم العرض القائم بمحل يمنع حلوله في المحل الثاني، مع أن مثل ذلك العرض، قد يكون قائماً بالمحل الثاني. فههنا المتماثلات في تمام الماهية، قد اختلفت في اللوازم. السادس: إن الإرادتين المعلقتين بالمراد الواحد، على الوجه الواحد: مثلان. ثم إن إحداهما قد تكون في محل، والأخرى لا في محل، والإرادة التي حصلت في المحل يمتنع حصولها لا في محل وبالعكس يمتنع. السابع: العرض الذي لا يبقى: يختص حدوثه بوقت معين. بمعنى أنه حدوثه إلا في الوقت الذي حدث فيه. ثم إن تلك الأعراض متماثلة كالأصوات متماثلة في تمام الماهية فههنا المساواة في تمام الماهية حاصلة، ولم يلزم منه المساواة في كل الأحكام. الثامن: عندهم العبد إذا فعل فعلا، ثم فني. فإنه لا يقدر على إعادته بعد العدم، وقد يقدر على إيجاد مثله. فههنا المتماثلات في تمام الماهية، لم تكن متساوية في كل الأحكام. التاسع: الوجود صفة واحدة عندهم. وأيضاً: فعندهم تأثير القادر ليس إلا في تحصيل الوجود. فإن الماهيات - عند مشايخهم - ثابتة في العدم، فيمتنع أن يكون للقادر فيها تأثير. ثم مع هذا، العبد يقدر على تحصيل الوجود لبعض الماهيات دون البعض. العاشر: الجوهر حال حدوثه مفتقر إلى الفاعل، وحال بقائه يمتنع إسناده إلى الفاعل والتفاوت بين الذات الواحدة: بحسب وقتين من التفاوت، بين ذاتين متفاوتين. فإذا جاز أن يختلف في الشخص الواحد بحسب وقتين، فيجوز أن يختلف في الشخص الواحد بحسب وقتين.
That specification is a condition for the occurrence of that judgment in one of the two sides, or a prevention from it in the other side? We concede: that specification is disregarded, but your statement: 'And what is valid for a thing is valid for its like' is refuted by many instances: First: The individuals of the final type are equal in complete quiddity, while the specification of this one prevents its occurrence for that one, and the specification of that one prevents its occurrence for this one. Otherwise, this would become that, and that would become this. All of that is impossible, so it is impossible that the power of the servant is like the power of God Almighty. Then it does not follow from that that the power of the servant is such that it is a power of God Almighty — according to the school of the Mu'tazila — because for them a single power between two agents is impossible. Third: The masters of the Mu'tazila: their school is that essences are equal in being essences, and they only differ from each other due to the specialization of each of them with its particular attribute. On this basis: the essence of God Almighty is equal to the rest of the essences in essentiality. Yet it does not follow from that that whatever is valid for each of the essences is also valid for the rest of the essences. Otherwise, it would follow that for the essence of God: temporality, contingency, need for a place, and that for the essences of originated things they are eternal, necessary of existence. All of that is impossible. Fourth: The attribute of existence — according to them — is one attribute. So the existence occurring in black and white is equal to the existence occurring in the essence of God. Yet equality in all judgments does not follow. Fifth: According to them, the accident subsisting in a place prevents its occurrence in a second place, while a similar accident may subsist in the second place. So here the similars in complete quiddity have differed in concomitant properties. Sixth: Two wills attached to one object of will in one aspect are similars. Yet one of them may be in a place and the other not in a place, and the will that occurred in a place is impossible to occur not in a place, and vice versa impossible. Seventh: The accident that does not persist: its coming into existence is confined to a specific time, meaning its coming into existence only at the time it occurred. Then these accidents are similar, just as sounds are similar in complete quiddity. So here equality in complete quiddity is present, yet equality in all judgments does not follow. Eighth: According to them, when the servant performs an act, then it perishes, he is not able to repeat it after non-existence, but may be able to produce its like. So here the similars in complete quiddity are not equal in all judgments. Ninth: Existence is one attribute according to them. Also: according to them, the effect of the agent is nothing but the acquisition of existence. For the quiddities — according to their masters — are established in non-existence, so it is impossible for the agent to have an effect on them. Yet, with this, the servant is able to acquire existence for some quiddities and not for others. Tenth: The substance at the moment of its occurrence is in need of the agent, but during its persistence it is impossible to attribute it to the agent. And the difference between one essence according to two times is like the difference between two different essences. So if it is permissible for one person to differ according to two times, then it is permissible for one person to differ according to two times.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a series of ten counterexamples (with the second omitted) aimed at refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that 'what is valid for a thing is valid for its like'. The author begins by conceding that specification (التعين) is irrelevant, but then argues that the Mu'tazilite principle is invalidated by many instances. The first example shows that individuals of a species are equal in quiddity, yet one cannot become the other because each has a specific identity. Applying this to divine power, the author argues that the servant's power cannot be like God's power, and refutes the Mu'tazilite notion that a single power could be shared between two agents. The third example points out that the Mu'tazilite masters themselves hold that essences are equal in essentiality, yet this does not permit ascribing temporal, contingent, or need-related attributes to God, nor ascribing eternity or necessity to creatures. The fourth example uses the attribute of existence: it is one, but the existence in accidents like black and white is not equal in all respects to God's existence. The fifth example notes that an accident subsisting in one location cannot subsist in another simultaneously, yet its similar can later subsist in that other location, so similars differ in concomitants. The sixth example discusses two wills attached to the same object: one may be in a subject and the other without a subject, and they cannot be exchanged. The seventh example deals with non-persisting accidents that are confined to a specific time, yet sounds are similar in quiddity but not equal in all rulings. The eighth example shows that a servant cannot repeat a perished act but can produce its like, so similars differ. The ninth example discusses existence as one attribute, yet the servant can acquire existence for some quiddities but not others, despite the quiddities being established in non-existence. The tenth example uses the substance: it depends on an agent at origination but not during persistence, so a single essence can differ over time, just as two different essences differ. The author concludes that difference in rulings does not require difference in essence.

Key Terms
الوجود
الماهية
الجوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the tenth example say about a substance's need for an agent?
A) It needs the agent always
B) It does not need the agent at all
C) It needs the agent at origination but not during persistence
D) It needs the agent only during persistence

Key Takeaway
A single essence can have different rulings over time, disproving the Mu'tazilite principle of equality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas in your life where you assume that because something depended on God at the start (like your faith or a relationship), it remains dependent in the same way—leaving no room for your own effort or change? Where do you act as if your current state is fixed, ignoring that the same essence can have different rulings at different times?
?
The ninth refutation shows that even though existence is one attribute from God, you can only acquire it for some things by His permission. In what pursuits are you trying to 'acquire existence'—success, reputation, or even righteousness—on your own terms, forgetting that the ability to act is itself a gift that can be withdrawn? Will you humbly acknowledge your dependence today?
?
The tenth refutation teaches that a single substance does not need the agent in the same way at all times. Think of a sin or bad habit you feel permanently trapped in. Is it possible that at a different time, with God's help, you could change? What would it mean to recognize that your essence can have different rulings, and that your past does not define your future?

Fifth Counterexample: Accidents and Locus Transfer
Razi presents the fifth counterexample involving accidents and their loci.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of accidents occupying loci demonstrates that entities identical in essence can differ in necessary properties.

Core Idea
Two identical accidents can differ in their ability to occupy a second locus.

The fifth counterexample zeroes in on accidents that subsist in a locus. According to the opponent, an accident prevents its occurrence in a second locus, yet a similar accident can occur there. This shows that similarity in essence does not guarantee equality in all judgments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the fifth counterexample. Notice the contrast between two similar accidents.
The Arabic uses 'مع أن' to indicate contrast.
2
Identify the two accidents
One accident prevents its occurrence in a second locus, while a similar accident can subsist in that second locus.
They are identical in essence but differ in a necessary property (the ability to transfer).
3
Understand the implication
This difference directly refutes the premise that similars in essence must be equal in all necessary properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : عندهم العرض القائم بمحل يمنع حلوله في المحل الثاني ، مع أن [ مثل (۱) ] ذلك العرض ، قد يكون قائماً بالمحل الثاني . فههنا المتماثلات في تمام الماهية ، قد اختلفت في اللوازم .
السادس : إن الإرادتين المعلقتين بالمراد الواحد ، على الوجه الواحد : مثلان . [ ثم (۲) ] إن إحداهما قد تكون في محل ، والأخرى لا في محل ، والإرادة التي حصلت في المحل يمتنع حصولها لا في محل وبالعكس يمتنع .
السابع : العرض الذي لا يبقى : يختص حدوثه بوقت معين . بمعنى أنه حدوثه إلا في الوقت الذي حدث فيه . ثم إن تلك الأعراض متماثلة كيا (۳) أن الأصوات متماثلة في تمام الماهية [ فههنا المساواة في تمام الماهية (4) ] حاصلة ، ولم يلزم منه المساواة في كل الأحكام .
الثامن : عندهم العبد إذا فعل فعلا ، ثم فني . فإنه لا يقدر على إعادته بعد العدم، وقد يقدر على إيجاد مثله . فههنا المتماثلات في تمام الماهية ، لم تكن متساوية في كل الأحكام .
التاسع : الوجود صفة واحدة عندهم . وأيضاً : فعندهم تأثير القادر ليس إلا في تحصيل الوجود . فإن الماهيات - عند مشايخهم - ثابتة في العدم ، فيمتنع أن يكون للقادر فيها تأثير . ثم مع هذا ، العبد يقدر على تحصيل الوجود لبعض الماهيات دون البعض .
العاشر : الجوهر حال حدوثه مفتقر إلى الفاعل ، وحال بقائه يمتنع إسناده إلى الفاعل والتفاوت بين الذات الواحدة : بحسب وقتين من التفاوت ، بين ذاتين متفاوتين. فإذا جاز أن يختلف في الشخص (٥) الواحد بحسب وقتين ، في الأحكام اللازمة ، فلم لا يجوز مثله في الذاتين المتفاوتين ؟ .
والحاصل : إن دليلهم على أن غير الله ، يمتنع أن يكون قادراً لذاته مبني على ثلاث مقدمات :
أولها : إن ذلك المغاير يجب أن يكون جسماً .
وثانيها : إن الأجسام متماثلة .
وثالثها : إن المتماثلات في تمام الماهية ، يجب تساويها في جميع اللوازم .
وقد ظهر : أن تنافي هذه المقدمات ، لم يتقرر البتة .
The fifth: According to them, an accident subsisting in a locus prevents its occurrence in a second locus, while [like (1)] that accident may be subsisting in a second locus. So here, similars in complete essence have differed in necessary properties.
The sixth: Two wills attached to one object in one respect are similars. [Then (2)] One of them may be in a locus, and the other not in a locus, and the will that occurs in a locus is impossible to occur not in a locus, and conversely it is impossible.
The seventh: The accident that does not persist: its occurrence is specific to a particular time. Meaning that its occurrence is only in the time in which it occurred. Then these accidents are similar, as sounds are similar in complete essence [so here equality in complete essence (4)] is present, and equality in all judgments does not follow from it.
The eighth: According to them, when a servant performs an action, then perishes, he is not able to reproduce it after non-existence, but he may be able to produce its like. So here, similars in complete essence are not equal in all judgments.
The ninth: Existence is one attribute according to them. And also: according to them, the effect of the powerful is only in obtaining existence. For essences—according to their masters—are fixed in non-existence, so it is impossible for the powerful to have effect on them. Then, with this, the servant is able to obtain existence for some essences but not others.
The tenth: The substance at the time of its occurrence is in need of an agent, and at the time of its persistence it is impossible to attribute it to the agent. The difference between one essence according to two times is like the difference between two different essences. So if it is permissible for one person (5) to differ according to two times in necessary properties, then why is the like not permissible in two different essences?
The summary: Their proof that other than God is impossible to be powerful by essence is based on three premises:
First: That other must be a body.
Second: That bodies are similar.
Third: That similars in complete essence must be equal in all necessary properties.
And it has become apparent that the contradiction of these premises has not been established at all.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents a series of six counterexamples (the fifth through tenth) to refute the opponent's principle that entities identical in essence must share all necessary properties. The underlying argument of the opponent is that only God can be powerful by essence; creatures cannot. That argument rests on three premises: (1) a non-God powerful by essence must be a body, (2) all bodies are similar, and (3) similars in essence must be equal in all necessary properties. The author (Razi) attacks the third premise by showing many cases where things similar in essence nonetheless differ in properties. Each of the six examples illustrates a different case: 
- Fifth: Accidents that can or cannot subsist in a second locus. 
- Sixth: Wills in a locus versus not in a locus. 
- Seventh: Non-persisting accidents occurring at a specific time. 
- Eighth: Actions of a servant who can reproduce the like but not the identical action. 
- Ninth: Existence as a single attribute, yet the servant can produce existence for some essences but not others. 
- Tenth: Substances at different times — a single entity can have different properties at different times, showing that similarity in essence does not necessitate equality in all properties. 
The conclusion is that the three premises have not been established; they are contradictory.

Key Terms
عَرَض (accident)
مَحَل (locus)
متماثلات (similars)
ماهية (essence)
لوازم (necessary properties)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that two accidents identical in essence must have exactly the same properties, such as the ability to occupy a locus.
Correction: The passage shows that two similar accidents can differ: one prevents its occurrence in a second locus, while another similar accident can occur there.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth counterexample, what do the two similar accidents differ in?
A) Their essence
B) Their ability to subsist in a second locus
C) Their duration
D) Their dependence on a creator

Key Takeaway
Identical essences can have different necessary properties, as shown by accidents and their loci.

Sixth Counterexample: Wills in and out of a Locus
Razi presents the sixth counterexample involving two wills.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two wills identical in essence can differ in whether they require a locus.

Core Idea
Two similar wills can differ: one exists in a locus, the other does not.

The sixth counterexample considers two wills attached to the same object in the same respect. They are similar in essence, yet one may be in a locus (e.g., a human) and the other not (e.g., in God). This shows that similarity does not require identical properties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the sixth counterexample carefully. Note the two wills are 'similars' but differ in locus.
The phrase 'مثلان' indicates they are similar in essence.
2
Compare their locations
One will is in a locus, the other is not. This is a necessary property difference.
A will in a locus cannot be not in a locus, and vice versa.
3
Draw the conclusion
Despite being similar in essence, they differ in necessary properties, refuting the opponent's premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وثالثها : إن المتماثلات في تمام الماهية ، يجب تساويها في جميع اللوازم .
وقد ظهر : أن تنافي هذه المقدمات ، لم يتقرر البتة .
The fifth: According to them, an accident subsisting in a locus prevents its occurrence in a second locus, while [like (1)] that accident may be subsisting in a second locus. So here, similars in complete essence have differed in necessary properties.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that two similar wills must have the same modal status regarding locus existence.
Correction: The passage shows that one will can be in a locus and the other not, despite being similar.

Checkpoint
In the sixth counterexample, what is the difference between the two similar wills?
A) One is eternal, the other temporal
B) One is in a locus, the other is not
C) One is stronger, the other weaker
D) They are identical in all respects

Key Takeaway
Two wills identical in essence can differ in whether they exist in a locus.

Seventh Counterexample: Non-Persisting Accidents and Time
Razi presents the seventh counterexample involving non-persisting accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how accidents that do not persist can be similar in essence but occur at distinct times.

Core Idea
Similar non-persisting accidents occur at specific times, differing in time of occurrence.

The seventh counterexample looks at accidents that do not persist, such as sounds. Although they are similar in essence, each occurs at a particular time and not at another. This shows that similarity in essence does not require equality in all judgments, such as time of occurrence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the seventh counterexample. Note that non-persisting accidents are similar but each is specific to its time.
The text mentions sounds as an example.
2
Understand the property
The necessary property here is the time of occurrence. Each accident can only happen at its specific time.
There is no persistence across times.
3
Apply the conclusion
Since these similar accidents differ in their time of occurrence, they differ in a necessary property, refuting the premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : عندهم العرض القائم بمحل يمنع حلوله في المحل الثاني ، مع أن [ مثل (۱) ] ذلك العرض ، قد يكون قائماً بالمحل الثاني . فههنا المتماثلات في تمام الماهية ، قد اختلفت في اللوازم .
السادس : إن الإرادتين المعلقتين بالمراد الواحد ، على الوجه الواحد : مثلان . [ ثم (۲) ] إن إحداهما قد تكون في محل ، والأخرى لا في محل ، والإرادة التي حصلت في المحل يمتنع حصولها لا في محل وبالعكس يمتنع .
السابع : العرض الذي لا يبقى : يختص حدوثه بوقت معين . بمعنى أنه حدوثه إلا في الوقت الذي حدث فيه . ثم إن تلك الأعراض متماثلة كيا (۳) أن الأصوات متماثلة في تمام الماهية [ فههنا المساواة في تمام الماهية (4) ] حاصلة ، ولم يلزم منه المساواة في كل الأحكام .
الثامن : عندهم العبد إذا فعل فعلا ، ثم فني . فإنه لا يقدر على إعادته بعد العدم، وقد يقدر على إيجاد مثله . فههنا المتماثلات في تمام الماهية ، لم تكن متساوية في كل الأحكام .
التاسع : الوجود صفة واحدة عندهم . وأيضاً : فعندهم تأثير القادر ليس إلا في تحصيل الوجود . فإن الماهيات - عند مشايخهم - ثابتة في العدم ، فيمتنع أن يكون للقادر فيها تأثير . ثم مع هذا ، العبد يقدر على تحصيل الوجود لبعض الماهيات دون البعض .
العاشر : الجوهر حال حدوثه مفتقر إلى الفاعل ، وحال بقائه يمتنع إسناده إلى الفاعل والتفاوت بين الذات الواحدة : بحسب وقتين من التفاوت ، بين ذاتين متفاوتين. فإذا جاز أن يختلف في الشخص (٥) الواحد بحسب وقتين ، في الأحكام اللازمة ، فلم لا يجوز مثله في الذاتين المتفاوتين ؟ .
والحاصل : إن دليلهم على أن غير الله ، يمتنع أن يكون قادراً لذاته مبني على ثلاث مقدمات :
أولها : إن ذلك المغاير يجب أن يكون جسماً .
وثانيها : إن الأجسام متماثلة .
وثالثها : إن المتماثلات في تمام الماهية ، يجب تساويها في جميع اللوازم .
وقد ظهر : أن تنافي هذه المقدمات ، لم يتقرر البتة .
The fifth: According to them, an accident subsisting in a locus prevents its occurrence in a second locus, while [like (1)] that accident may be subsisting in a second locus. So here, similars in complete essence have differed in necessary properties.
The sixth: Two wills attached to one object in one respect are similars. [Then (2)] One of them may be in a locus, and the other not in a locus, and the will that occurs in a locus is impossible to occur not in a locus, and conversely it is impossible.
The seventh: The accident that does not persist: its occurrence is specific to a particular time. Meaning that its occurrence is only in the time in which it occurred. Then these accidents are similar, as sounds are similar in complete essence [so here equality in complete essence (4)] is present, and equality in all judgments does not follow from it.
The eighth: According to them, when a servant performs an action, then perishes, he is not able to reproduce it after non-existence, but he may be able to produce its like. So here, similars in complete essence are not equal in all judgments.
The ninth: Existence is one attribute according to them. And also: according to them, the effect of the powerful is only in obtaining existence. For essences—according to their masters—are fixed in non-existence, so it is impossible for the powerful to have effect on them. Then, with this, the servant is able to obtain existence for some essences but not others.
The tenth: The substance at the time of its occurrence is in need of an agent, and at the time of its persistence it is impossible to attribute it to the agent. The difference between one essence according to two times is like the difference between two different essences. So if it is permissible for one person (5) to differ according to two times in necessary properties, then why is the like not permissible in two different essences?
The summary: Their proof that other than God is impossible to be powerful by essence is based on three premises:
First: That other must be a body.
Second: That bodies are similar.
Third: That similars in complete essence must be equal in all necessary properties.
And it has become apparent that the contradiction of these premises has not been established at all.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents a series of six counterexamples (the fifth through tenth) to refute the opponent's principle that entities identical in essence must share all necessary properties. The underlying argument of the opponent is that only God can be powerful by essence; creatures cannot. That argument rests on three premises: (1) a non-God powerful by essence must be a body, (2) all bodies are similar, and (3) similars in essence must be equal in all necessary properties. The author (Razi) attacks the third premise by showing many cases where things similar in essence nonetheless differ in properties. Each of the six examples illustrates a different case: 
- Fifth: Accidents that can or cannot subsist in a second locus. 
- Sixth: Wills in a locus versus not in a locus. 
- Seventh: Non-persisting accidents occurring at a specific time. 
- Eighth: Actions of a servant who can reproduce the like but not the identical action. 
- Ninth: Existence as a single attribute, yet the servant can produce existence for some essences but not others. 
- Tenth: Substances at different times — a single entity can have different properties at different times, showing that similarity in essence does not necessitate equality in all properties. 
The conclusion is that the three premises have not been established; they are contradictory.

Key Terms
عَرَض (accident)
حدوث (occurrence)
وقت (time)
متماثلات (similars)
أصوات (sounds)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that if two sounds are identical in essence, they must occur at the same time or have the same duration.
Correction: The passage shows that similar sounds occur at different times, differing in that property.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh counterexample, what property do similar non-persisting accidents differ in?
A) Their essence
B) Their time of occurrence
C) Their location
D) Their dependence on a creator

Key Takeaway
Similar non-persisting accidents differ in their necessary property of temporal occurrence.

Eighth Counterexample: Servant’s Action and Its Like
Razi presents the eighth counterexample regarding a servant's action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a servant’s action and its like can be similar in essence but differ in reproducibility.

Core Idea
A servant can reproduce a similar action but not the exact same action.

The eighth counterexample uses the example of a servant who performs an action that then perishes. The servant cannot reproduce that exact action after it ceases, but he can produce another similar action. Hence two similar actions differ in the property of being the same particular action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the eighth counterexample. Note the distinction between reproducing the identical action versus its like.
2
Identify the difference
Two actions similar in essence: one is the original, the other a new similar action. They differ in the property of being the same particular.
3
Understand the implication
This shows that similars in essence are not equal in all judgments (here, the ability to be reproduced identically).

Source Text

In the eighth counterexample, what can the servant not do after the action perishes?
A) Reproduce the exact same action
B) Produce any similar action
C) Remember the action
D) Cause the action to persist

Key Takeaway
Similar actions can differ in being the same individual, thus not all properties are shared.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider an action you performed that you deeply regret and wish you could undo. According to this lesson, you cannot reproduce that exact action—not because you lack power, but because the specific instance is gone forever. How does this realization affect your view of repentance and the finality of your deeds? Are you taking your present actions seriously enough, knowing that they are unique and cannot be re-created?
?
Razi shows that even when you can produce a similar action, it is not the same. Think of a good deed you repeated many times, like giving charity. Have you ever assumed that because you've done something similar before, you have already 'earned' that reward? How does the fact that each action is a distinct individual with its own judgment challenge your complacency?
?
The lesson distinguishes between the ability to produce a like versus the inability to reproduce the same. In your spiritual life, do you often deceive yourself into thinking that a similar effort will suffice for a unique obligation? Identify one duty you've been postponing, convincing yourself that you can do something similar later. What is one thing you can do today to act on the unique opportunity now?

Ninth Counterexample: Existence and the Servant's Power
Razi presents the ninth counterexample involving existence and the servant's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the attribute of existence can be produced by the servant for some essences but not others, though existence is one.

Core Idea
Existence is one attribute, yet the servant can produce it for some essences and not others.

The ninth counterexample deals with the nature of existence. According to the opponent, existence is a single attribute, and the powerful (including the servant) can only produce existence. But essences are fixed in non-existence. Yet the servant can produce existence for some essences but not others. This shows that existence, though one, is not granted equally to all essences, refuting the premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the ninth counterexample. Note that existence is considered one attribute, and the effect of the powerful is only in producing existence.
2
Recognize the contradiction
If existence is one, why can the servant produce it for some essences but not others? This difference is a necessary property.
3
Understand the refutation
The difference in the ability to receive existence shows that similar essences (in being essences) differ in a necessary property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
According to the ninth counterexample, what can the servant do for some essences but not others?
A) Change their essence
B) Obtain existence for them
C) Destroy them
D) Make them eternal

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the existence of anything you have achieved—your skills, relationships, or possessions—do you truly believe that your power is limited to merely obtaining existence for what was already fixed? How does this reshape your view of your own ability and dependence on Allah?
?
If all essences are equal in their fixed non-existence, why has Allah chosen to grant you existence and not others? What does this teach you about His mercy and your responsibility?
?
Think of a time you failed to bring something into existence despite your efforts. According to Razi, that failure is not because your power was lacking over the essence, but because that essence was not meant to receive existence from you. How can this understanding increase your humility and trust in Allah's decree?

Tenth Counterexample: Substance’s Dependence at Different Times
Razi presents the tenth counterexample involving a substance and its dependence on an agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single substance can have different necessary properties at different times, undermining the opponent’s premise.

Core Idea
A substance in need of an agent at one time can be independent later.

The tenth counterexample focuses on a substance: at the moment of its occurrence, it requires an agent, but while persisting it does not. This difference in a single entity over time shows that even one essence can have differing necessary properties. If that is allowed, then why can't two similar essences also differ?

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the tenth counterexample. Note the comparison between a single substance at two times.
2
Analyze the difference
The substance at the time of occurrence is in need of an agent, but at persistence it is not attributed to an agent.
This is a difference in necessary property over time.
3
Apply the analogy
If a single essence can differ in properties over time, then two similar essences can also differ in properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : عندهم العرض القائم بمحل يمنع حلوله في المحل الثاني ، مع أن [ مثل (۱) ] ذلك العرض ، قد يكون قائماً بالمحل الثاني . فههنا المتماثلات في تمام الماهية ، قد اختلفت في اللوازم .
السادس : إن الإرادتين المعلقتين بالمراد الواحد ، على الوجه الواحد : مثلان . [ ثم (۲) ] إن إحداهما قد تكون في محل ، والأخرى لا في محل ، والإرادة التي حصلت في المحل يمتنع حصولها لا في محل وبالعكس يمتنع .
السابع : العرض الذي لا يبقى : يختص حدوثه بوقت معين . بمعنى أنه حدوثه إلا في الوقت الذي حدث فيه . ثم إن تلك الأعراض متماثلة كيا (۳) أن الأصوات متماثلة في تمام الماهية [ فههنا المساواة في تمام الماهية (4) ] حاصلة ، ولم يلزم منه المساواة في كل الأحكام .
الثامن : عندهم العبد إذا فعل فعلا ، ثم فني . فإنه لا يقدر على إعادته بعد العدم، وقد يقدر على إيجاد مثله . فههنا المتماثلات في تمام الماهية ، لم تكن متساوية في كل الأحكام .
التاسع : الوجود صفة واحدة عندهم . وأيضاً : فعندهم تأثير القادر ليس إلا في تحصيل الوجود . فإن الماهيات - عند مشايخهم - ثابتة في العدم ، فيمتنع أن يكون للقادر فيها تأثير . ثم مع هذا ، العبد يقدر على تحصيل الوجود لبعض الماهيات دون البعض .
العاشر : الجوهر حال حدوثه مفتقر إلى الفاعل ، وحال بقائه يمتنع إسناده إلى الفاعل والتفاوت بين الذات الواحدة : بحسب وقتين من التفاوت ، بين ذاتين متفاوتين. فإذا جاز أن يختلف في الشخص (٥) الواحد بحسب وقتين ، في الأحكام اللازمة ، فلم لا يجوز مثله في الذاتين المتفاوتين ؟ .
والحاصل : إن دليلهم على أن غير الله ، يمتنع أن يكون قادراً لذاته مبني على ثلاث مقدمات :
أولها : إن ذلك المغاير يجب أن يكون جسماً .
وثانيها : إن الأجسام متماثلة .
وثالثها : إن المتماثلات في تمام الماهية ، يجب تساويها في جميع اللوازم .
وقد ظهر : أن تنافي هذه المقدمات ، لم يتقرر البتة .
The fifth: According to them, an accident subsisting in a locus prevents its occurrence in a second locus, while [like (1)] that accident may be subsisting in a second locus. So here, similars in complete essence have differed in necessary properties.
The sixth: Two wills attached to one object in one respect are similars. [Then (2)] One of them may be in a locus, and the other not in a locus, and the will that occurs in a locus is impossible to occur not in a locus, and conversely it is impossible.
The seventh: The accident that does not persist: its occurrence is specific to a particular time. Meaning that its occurrence is only in the time in which it occurred. Then these accidents are similar, as sounds are similar in complete essence [so here equality in complete essence (4)] is present, and equality in all judgments does not follow from it.
The eighth: According to them, when a servant performs an action, then perishes, he is not able to reproduce it after non-existence, but he may be able to produce its like. So here, similars in complete essence are not equal in all judgments.
The ninth: Existence is one attribute according to them. And also: according to them, the effect of the powerful is only in obtaining existence. For essences—according to their masters—are fixed in non-existence, so it is impossible for the powerful to have effect on them. Then, with this, the servant is able to obtain existence for some essences but not others.
The tenth: The substance at the time of its occurrence is in need of an agent, and at the time of its persistence it is impossible to attribute it to the agent. The difference between one essence according to two times is like the difference between two different essences. So if it is permissible for one person (5) to differ according to two times in necessary properties, then why is the like not permissible in two different essences?
The summary: Their proof that other than God is impossible to be powerful by essence is based on three premises:
First: That other must be a body.
Second: That bodies are similar.
Third: That similars in complete essence must be equal in all necessary properties.
And it has become apparent that the contradiction of these premises has not been established at all.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents a series of six counterexamples (the fifth through tenth) to refute the opponent's principle that entities identical in essence must share all necessary properties. The underlying argument of the opponent is that only God can be powerful by essence; creatures cannot. That argument rests on three premises: (1) a non-God powerful by essence must be a body, (2) all bodies are similar, and (3) similars in essence must be equal in all necessary properties. The author (Razi) attacks the third premise by showing many cases where things similar in essence nonetheless differ in properties. Each of the six examples illustrates a different case: 
- Fifth: Accidents that can or cannot subsist in a second locus. 
- Sixth: Wills in a locus versus not in a locus. 
- Seventh: Non-persisting accidents occurring at a specific time. 
- Eighth: Actions of a servant who can reproduce the like but not the identical action. 
- Ninth: Existence as a single attribute, yet the servant can produce existence for some essences but not others. 
- Tenth: Substances at different times — a single entity can have different properties at different times, showing that similarity in essence does not necessitate equality in all properties. 
The conclusion is that the three premises have not been established; they are contradictory.

Key Terms
جوهر (substance)
فاعل (agent)
حدوث (occurrence)
بقاء (persistence)
ذات (essence)
وقت (time)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that a substance must have the same properties at all times, or that two similar substances must have identical properties.
Correction: The passage shows that a single substance can have different properties at different times, so two similar substances can also differ.

Checkpoint
According to the tenth counterexample, what is the difference in a substance between the time of occurrence and the time of persistence?
A) At occurrence it is need of an agent; at persistence it is not
B) At occurrence it is eternal; at persistence it is temporal
C) At occurrence it is a body; at persistence it is not
D) They are identical in all respects

Key Takeaway
A single substance can have different necessary properties at different times, showing that similarity does not require equality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged your spiritual state as fixed because your outward circumstances seemed similar to another time? How does Razi's argument about a substance changing in necessary properties over time challenge your assumption of spiritual stability?
?
Consider a moment when you felt completely dependent on Allah, and another when you felt self-sufficient. Does that difference in your state mean your faith essence has changed? What does this lesson teach you about the diversity of your own soul?
?
If two people share the same faith at one moment, can you accept that they may differ in necessary attributes (like closeness to Allah) at another? How does Razi's analogy free you from expecting uniformity in others?

Summary: Three Premises Refuted
Razi summarizes the opponent's three premises and declares them unestablished.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi concludes that the opponent's three premises for denying creaturely power are not established.

Core Idea
The opponent's proof fails because its premises are not proven.

The summary paragraph recapitulates the opponent's argument that only God can be powerful by essence, based on three premises: (1) the powerful being must be a body, (2) bodies are similar, and (3) similars must be equal in all necessary properties. Razi declares that these premises have not been established; the counterexamples show that premise 3 is false, thus the proof collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the summary passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the three premises listed.
2
Understand the conclusion
Razi states that the contradiction (i.e., the inconsistency) of these premises has not been established at all.
This means the opponent's argument is unsound.
3
Connect to previous counterexamples
The six counterexamples directly attack the third premise, showing it is false.

?

?

?

Refuting the Argument That Only God Can Have Power by a Power
Razi refutes three premises of an argument that only God can be powerful by a power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the claim that a power insufficient for creating a body must share a common cause.

Core Idea
Common impossibility does not prove a common cause; non-existence has no cause.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi continues his critical examination of the arguments for attributing power exclusively to God. He turns to a second line of reasoning that attempts to prove that only God can be powerful by a power (قدرة). The opponent argues that all created powers that are unable to create a body must share a common cause for that inability. Razi dissects this argument into three premises and refutes each one, using logical and theological insights. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the structure: the opponent's claim and Razi's three refutations.
2
Identify the three premises
Razi lists three premises of the opponent that he refutes. Find each one and note how he counters it.
First premise: impossibility must have a cause. Second: common judgment needs common cause. Third: only commonality is being a power.
3
Understand the refutation of the first premise
Razi argues that impossibility is pure non-existence, and non-existence has no cause. Reflect on why this is a powerful critique.
If impossibility were a real attribute, it might require a cause, but Razi denies its reality.
4
Grasp the Mu'tazilite example for the second premise
Razi gives the example of ugliness (قبح) from Mu'tazilite theology. Different actions are ugly for different specific reasons, not a single common cause.
This shows that a common judgment (ugliness) can arise from diverse causes, refuting the need for a common cause.
5
Consider the third premise and the concluding point
Razi denies that the only commonality among incapable powers is being a power. Powers could have various attributes. Our ignorance of these attributes does not prove they don't exist.
The argument ends with a reminder: lack of knowledge is not proof of non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني : لم لا يجوز أن يكون قادراً بالقدوة ؟
أما قوله : و القدرة الحاصلة ، عندما لا يصلح شيء منها ، لإيجاد الجسم . فهذا الامتناع حكم مشترك ، فلا بد من علة مشتركة (۱) ولا مشترك إلا كونها قدراً .
قلنا : هذا الكلام أيضاً مبني على أربع مقدمات :
أولها : قوله : « هذا الامتناع لا بد له من علة ، وهذا باطل لأن الامتناع عدم محض ، والعدم لا علة له .
وثانيها : قوله : ( الحكم المشترك لا بد له من علة مشتركة ، وهذا أيضاً باطل . بدليل : أن القبح عند المعتزلة ، وصف مشترك فيه بين الظلم والجهل ، والكذب والعبث . ثم إن قبح الظلم عندهم معلل بخصوص كونه ظلماً ، وقبح الجهل معلل بخصوص كونه جهلا . فهذا حكم مشترك فيه . وهو غير معلل بعلة مشتركة فيها .
وثالثها : قوله : و لا شركة إلا كونها قدراً ، وهذا أيضاً ممنوع . فلم لا يجوز أن يقال القدر على قسمين قسم لا يصلح لخلق الجسم ، وقسم يصلح لذلك . والقسم الذي لا يصلح لخلق الجسم : مشترك في وصف واحد ، ولأجل ذلك الوصف يتعذر فعل الجسم بها . وذلك الوصف غير حاصل في القسم الثاني ؟ وأقصى ما في الباب : أنا لا نعرف ذلك . لكن عدم العلم .
The second station: Why is it not permissible that [something] be powerful by a power?
As for his statement: 'And the acquired power, when not any of it is suitable for creating a body, then this impossibility is a common judgment, so there must be a common cause (1) and there is no commonality except its being a power.' We say: This speech is also based on four premises:
First: His statement: 'This impossibility must have a cause.' And this is false because impossibility is pure non-existence, and non-existence has no cause.
Second: His statement: 'The common judgment must have a common cause.' This is also false. The proof: Ugliness according to the Mu'tazila is a common description between injustice, ignorance, lying, and frivolity. Then the ugliness of injustice is, according to them, caused by its specificity of being injustice, and the ugliness of ignorance is caused by its specificity of being ignorance. So this is a common judgment, and it is not caused by a common cause.
Third: His statement: 'And there is no commonality except its being a power.' This is also denied. For why is it not permissible to say that power is of two kinds: a kind not suitable for creating a body, and a kind suitable for that? And the kind that is not suitable for creating a body: it is common in one attribute, and on account of that attribute the action of the body is impossible by it. And that attribute is not present in the second kind? The utmost in this matter: we do not know that. But lack of knowledge...
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage addresses a second line of argument: why can't a non-God be powerful by a power (قدرة) rather than by essence? The opponent argues that if a power is insufficient to create a body, then this insufficiency is a common property, and all such powers must share a common cause for their inability, and the only commonality is that they are powers. Razi refutes this by analyzing four premises:
First: The claim that impossibility must have a cause is false because impossibility is pure non-existence (عدم), and non-existence has no cause.
Second: The claim that a common judgment requires a common cause is false, as shown by the example of ugliness (قبح) according to the Mu'tazila: different actions (injustice, ignorance, lying, frivolity) are ugly for different specific reasons, not a single common cause. Thus a common judgment can arise from disparate causes.
Third: The claim that the only commonality is being a power is denied: powers may be divided into different kinds with different attributes. The kind unsuitable for creating a body may have a specific attribute that prevents creation, and that attribute is absent in the suitable kind. 
The passage ends with the observation that our ignorance of such a distinction does not prove its non-existence. The argument thus fails to establish that only God can be powerful by a power.

Key Terms
قدرة (power)
امتناع (impossibility)
عدة (cause)
حكم مشترك (common judgment)
عدم (non-existence)
قبح (ugliness)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that impossibility, as a common property, must have a single cause.
Correction: Razi clarifies that impossibility is non-existence, which requires no cause, and common judgments can arise from multiple specific causes.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject the first premise of the opponent's argument about impossibility?
A) Because impossibility is an attribute of God
B) Because impossibility is pure non-existence and has no cause
C) Because the opponent's argument is circular
D) Because impossibility requires a specific cause

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that a common property, especially a negation like impossibility, does not necessitate a single cause; diverse specifics can produce similar judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your inability to do something good is because you are simply 'not powerful enough' – attributing it to a single common flaw – instead of examining the specific reason for each failure? How does Razi's lesson free you from that lazy assumption?
?
When you see someone else struggling with a task, do you rush to judge that they share a common weakness with other failures? Razi shows that a common judgment does not require a common cause. Will you now pause to consider the unique factors in their situation before assuming?
?
Razi says non-existence has no cause. Your past sins are now non-existent – forgiven and erased. Do you still hold onto guilt as if they have an enduring cause that defines you? Or will you accept that their non-existence truly has no lingering effect on who you are in Allah's sight?

Refuting the Mu'tazila's Argument on Divine Essence and Contingency
The author presents and refutes the Mu'tazila's third argument that God's essence must be contingent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila's argument that God's essence must be contingent is invalid and how it leads to the collapse of the miracle as proof.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's logic forces God to share in contingency—an absurdity that refutes their own position.

The Mu'tazila argued that if a servant can create some events, then God's essence must be contingent like all created essences. In this passage, the author exposes the logical flaw: even if God's essence differs from creation, it would still share in contingency, change, and possibility—which is impossible for God. This refutation also shows the broader consequence that allowing any creation by a servant undermines the evidential value of miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the dilemma structure: God's essence is either equal or different from created essences.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's premise
The Mu'tazila assume that if a non-God can create some events, then God's essence must have the same properties as those events. Find where this premise appears in the passage.
3
Understand the author's refutation
The author presents a dilemma and shows that both horns lead to absurdity. Follow the reasoning: even if God's essence is different, it would still share in contingency, possibility, and change.
The author declares the argument false and then draws a broader consequence about miracles.
4
Reflect on the consequence for miracles
The author concludes that if a servant can create some events, he must be able to create all events, which would nullify the miracle as proof of truthfulness. Consider how this refutes the Mu'tazila's position.
This ties back to the overall argument about divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يصح الثالث : إن هذا الكلام يلزم عليه أن يصح على ذات الله تعالى ، كل ما على كل الحوادث . فإنه يقال : ذات الله تعالى . إما أن تكون مساوية لهذه الذوات ، أو تكون مخالفة لها . فإن كان الأول ، وجب أن يصح عليه تعالى كل ما يصح على هذه الحوادث. وإن كان الثاني لم تكن مخالفة ذاته لهذه الذوات ، إلا كمخالفة بعض هذه الحوادث. وإن كان الثاني لم تكن مخالفة ذاته لهذه الذوات ، إلا كمخالفة بعض هذه الذوات لبعض . فكما أن هذه الذوات مخالفة بعضها لبعض ، متشاركة في الحدوث والإمكان والتغير ، وجب أن تصح كل هذه الأمور على ذات الله تعالى. ولما كان هذا الكلام باطلاً ، فكذا ما ذكرتم . وبالله التوفيق فهذا حكاية عمدة المعتزلة. في أن غير الله ، لا يقدر على فعل الجسم ، وعلى فعل الحياة . وقد ظهر ضعفه وسقوطه . فثبت : أنهم لما جوزوا كون العبد موجداً لبعض الحوادث، لزمهم تجويز كونه موجداً لكلها ، ومتى جوزوا ذلك خرج المعجز عن كونه دليلاً على الصدق .
The third [point] is valid: This speech necessarily entails that what applies to all contingent events should apply to the essence of Allah, the Exalted. For it is said: The essence of Allah, the Exalted, either is equal to these essences, or is different from them. If it were the first, then everything that applies to these contingent events must apply to Him, the Exalted. And if it were the second, His essence's difference from these essences would only be like the difference of some of these contingent events [from others]. And if it were the second, His essence's difference from these essences would only be like the difference of some of these essences from others. And just as these essences differ from each other while sharing in contingency ([حدث]), possibility ([إمكان]), and change ([تغير]), so too all these matters must necessarily apply to the essence of Allah, the Exalted. And since this speech is false, so too is what you have mentioned. And with Allah is success. So this is the narration of the main argument of the Mu'tazila, concerning that other than Allah is not able to create the body ([الجسم]) nor to create life ([الحياة]). And its weakness and fall have become apparent. So it is established that when they permitted that the servant can be the creator ([موجد]) of some events, they necessarily permitted that he can be the creator of all of them; and whenever they permitted that, the miracle ([معجز]) ceases to be evidence ([دليل]) of truthfulness ([صدق]).
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of a Mu'tazilite argument. The author begins by stating the third argument of the Mu'tazila: that if a non-God can create some events, then it must be possible for the essence of God to have all the properties of created essences, such as contingency. The argument is presented as a dilemma: God's essence is either equal to created essences or different. If equal, then all that applies to created essences applies to God. If different, then the difference is only like the difference among created essences themselves. But created essences, despite their differences, share the general properties of being contingent, possible, and changing. Therefore, even if God's essence is different, those properties would still apply. The author then declares this speech false, and concludes that the Mu'tazila's position is invalid. The author sums up that the Mu'tazila claim that non-God cannot create bodies or life, but their own argument leads to the consequence that if the servant can create some events, he must be able to create all events, which would remove the miracle's power as proof of prophetic truthfulness.

Correction: The author is exposing an absurd consequence of their position, not endorsing it. The argument is presented to refute it.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the necessary consequence if one allows that a servant can create some events?

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's argument fails because it forces God to be contingent, which is impossible; allowing any creation by a servant destroys the proof of prophethood.

?
This lesson shows that allowing any creation by a servant destroys the proof of prophethood. Do you truly believe that only Allah creates your successes, your health, your provision? Or do you, in practice, credit yourself or others as 'creators' of these outcomes? Will you repent of that hidden shirk right now?
?
If your faith in Allah's uniqueness depends on arguments that could be refuted, what is its foundation? Has this lesson exposed a weak spot in your aqeedah that you have been ignoring? What step will you take to strengthen it today?

Refutation of Six Interpretations of Divine Misguidance
The author refutes six allegorical interpretations of a verse about divine misguidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why six allegorical interpretations of the verse about deviated hearts are rationally and textually inadequate, and why ta'wil must be supported by evidence.

Core Idea
Allegorical interpretation must be justified by evidence, not mere possibility.

In this passage, the author systematically dismantles six alternative readings of the verse about divine misguidance. Each ta'wil is shown to be either internally inconsistent or to contradict the purpose of supplication. The lesson highlights the principle that ta'wil is only permissible when the literal meaning is demonstrably impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of the six refutations and the concluding principle.
Pay attention to the structure: each ta'wil is introduced with 'أما'.
2
Understand the first two ta'wils
Note the argument that if something is divinely obligatory, supplication is pointless; and that divine assistance in duty is either harmful or useless.
The first ta'wil is weak because it makes supplication redundant; the second because God's action would be either evil or ineffective.
3
Analyze the fourth ta'wil
See the reductio ad absurdum regarding God's foreknowledge: if foreknowledge of sin required immediate death, then foreknowledge of lifelong sin would require non-creation.
This shows that the fourth interpretation leads to an absurd conclusion.
4
Examine the fifth and sixth ta'wils
The fifth is refuted by context (the verse is connected to previous verses about deviation). The sixth is refuted by the same logic as the first: if possible, it is obligatory; if not, supplication is futile.
Note the pattern: beneficial interpretations that are within God's power would make supplication pointless.
5
Grasp the concluding principle
The author states that ta'wil is only proper when the literal meaning is impossible to apply, which is not the case here. Thus, resorting to these interpretations is unjustified.
This principle is key to understanding when allegorical interpretation is valid in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب :
أما التأويل الأول : فضعيف . لأن مذهبهم : أن كل ما أمكن في قدرة الله تعالى أن يفعل في حقهم من الألطاف ، فقد وجب ذلك وجوباً ، لو ترك لبطلت الهيته ، ولصار جاهلاً أو محتاجاً. والشيء الذي يكون ، فأي حاجة إلى الدعاء والتضرع في طلبه ؟ وأما الثاني : فضعيف أيضاً . لأن التسديد في التكليف . إن علم الله أن له أثراً في حمل المكلف على القبيح ، كان فعله من الله قبيحاً . وإن علم أنه لا أثر له في حمل المكلف على فعل القبيح ، كان وجوده كعدمه ، فيما يرجع إلى كون العبد مطيعاً وعاصيا . فلا فائدة في صرف الدعاء إليه
وأما الثالث : وهو أن يكون المراد أن لا يوقع الله علينا اسم الزيغ . فقد تقدم جوابه
وأما الرابع : فجوابه : أنه لو كان علمه بأنه يكفر في السنة الثانية ، يوجب عليه أن يميته في الحال ، لكان علمه بأنه يكفر طول عمره ، يوجب عليه أن لا يخلقه البتة
وأما الخامس : وهو حمله على إبقاء العقل . فضعيف . لأن هذه الآية متعلقة بما هو مذكور قبل هذه الآية . وهو قوله : ( فأما الذين في قلوبهم زيع ) .
وأما السادس : وهو أن يحمل ذلك على الحراسة من الشيطان ، ومن شرور النفس . فنقول : ذلك إن كان مقدوراً لله ، فقد وجب فعله ، فلا فائدة في طلبه . وإن لم يكن مقدوراً فلا فائدة في الدعاء . فظهر بما ذكرنا : سقوط جملة هذه الوجوه .
ثم نقول : المصير إلى هذه التأويلات . إنما يحسن إذا دل الدليل على تعذر إجراء هذا النص ، فهو الحق الصريح. وإذا كان كذلك ، فكيف يصار فيه إلى التأويل .
So the answer: As for the first ta'wil: it is weak. Because their doctrine is that everything that is possible in the power of God Almighty to do as favors for them is necessarily obligatory; if He omitted it, His divinity would be invalidated, and He would become ignorant or needy. And if a thing is certain, what need is there for supplication and earnest prayer in seeking it? As for the second: it is also weak. Because assistance in the obligation, if God knows that it has an effect in deterring the obligated person from evil, then His doing it would be evil from God. And if He knows that it has no effect in deterring the obligated person from doing evil, then its existence is like its non-existence regarding the servant being obedient or disobedient. So there is no benefit in directing supplication to Him. As for the third: which is that the intended meaning is that God should not impose on us the name of deviation—its answer has already preceded. As for the fourth: its answer is that if His knowledge that he will disbelieve in the second year necessitated that He should kill him immediately, then His knowledge that he will disbelieve throughout his life would necessitate that He should not create him at all. As for the fifth: which is interpreting it as preserving the intellect, it is weak. Because this verse is connected to what is mentioned before this verse, namely His statement: 'As for those in whose hearts is deviation' (3:7). As for the sixth: which is that it should be interpreted as protection from Satan and from the evils of the soul. We say: if that is possible for God, then its doing is obligatory, so there is no benefit in seeking it. And if it is not possible, then there is no benefit in supplication. So it has become clear from what we have mentioned: the fall of all these interpretations. Then we say: resorting to these ta'wils is only proper if there is evidence indicating the impossibility of applying this text literally, for it is the clear truth. And if that is the case, how can one resort to ta'wil in it?
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of six allegorical interpretations (ta'wils) of a verse about God's misguidance (likely referring to 'those in whose hearts is deviation' in Quran 3:7). The author systematically rejects each ta'wil, showing that they either undermine the purpose of supplication, lead to absurd consequences, or ignore the textual context. The first ta'wil (that the verse refers to God's necessary favors) is weak because if something is divinely obligatory, supplication becomes pointless. The second ta'wil (about divine assistance in duty) fails because if God's help influences obedience, then withholding it would be evil; if it has no effect, it is useless. The third ta'wil (that the verse asks God not to label us as deviant) is already answered earlier. The fourth ta'wil (that God's foreknowledge of unbelief necessitates immediate death) is refuted by a reductio: if foreknowledge of future sin requires immediate death, then foreknowledge of lifelong sin would require non-creation. The fifth ta'wil (that the verse is about preserving intellect) ignores the immediate context of the verse. The sixth ta'wil (about protection from Satan and the soul) again makes supplication redundant if God can protect, and futile if He cannot. The author concludes that all these interpretations fail, and that resorting to ta'wil is only justified when the literal meaning is evidently impossible—which is not the case here.

الحاجة إلى الدعاء
التسديد

إبقاء العقل
الحراسة من الشيطان

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any allegorical interpretation is valid as long as it is possible.
Correction: The author shows that ta'wil must be supported by evidence, not mere possibility, and that the literal meaning must be evidently impossible before resorting to allegory.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the first ta'wil?
A) It contradicts the Quran.
B) It makes supplication pointless.
C) It implies God is ignorant.
D) It ignores the context.

Key Takeaway
The literal meaning of the verse is rationally sound; allegorical interpretation requires strong evidence, not mere possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a Quranic verse that seems to conflict with your understanding of God's justice, do you tend to search for an allegorical interpretation that fits your expectations, or do you wrestle with the literal meaning as the author insists? What evidence do you bring for your interpretation?
?
The author argues that if God's help is certain, supplication is pointless—but you still pray. In what ways does your prayer reflect trust that God will act regardless, and in what ways do you pray as if your asking makes a difference? How does this tension challenge your daily practice?
?
Consider a disagreement you have with others about a religious text. Are you quick to adopt an interpretation that justifies your position, or do you demand evidence that the literal meaning is impossible? How would the author's standard for ta'wil change how you approach such disagreements?

Objection and Answer: The Reading and Attribution of Good and Evil
Responds to two objections regarding divine attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars refuted objections based on irregular Quranic readings and the multiple meanings of divine attribution.

Core Idea
Irregular readings do not undermine authentic theology; divine attribution distinguishes creation from enablement.

This passage addresses two objections that challenge the Ash'arite view that all actions, whether good or evil, are created by Allah. The first objection cites an irregular reading of a verse, while the second points to a verse that attributes good deeds to Allah. The author systematically shows why the irregular reading is invalid and clarifies the semantics of attribution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its two aspects
Read the opening objection carefully. It has two parts: first, an irregular reading of a verse; second, a claim that the Mu'tazilites use a verse attributing good to Allah to argue that all acts come from servants.
Note that the objection is posed as 'it is not said' (ولا يقال) which introduces a potential counterargument.
2
Understand the response to the first objection
The author gives two reasons why the irregular reading is rejected: (1) it is abandoned and considered a distortion of the Quran; (2) the mutawatir reading is sound and supports his position. If the irregular reading contradicts the mutawatir, it is definitively rejected; otherwise, it is irrelevant.
Focus on the distinction between mutawatir and irregular (shadhdh) readings.
3
Examine the response to the second objection
The author explains that when something is attributed to someone, it can mean either they created it or they enabled it. This distinction is key to understanding the verses.
4
Apply the distinction to 'sayyi'ah' and note the incomplete explanation for 'hasanah'
For 'sayyi'ah' (evil) being from the servant, it cannot mean enabled because the servant lacks the ability for sin; therefore it must mean the servant creates it. For 'hasanah' (good) from Allah, it could mean either creation or enablement, but the text cuts off before completing.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the author likely intended to clarify that both meanings are possible for good deeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقال : هذا مدفوع من وجهين : الأول : إن بعضهم قرأ ( فمن نفسك ) ؟ الثاني : إنه تعالى أضاف الحسنة إلى نفسه ، حيث قال : ( ما أصابك من حسنة فمن الله ) تعالى ، وعند المعتزلة : أن الطاعات والمعاصي كلها من العباد - لأنا نقول : أما الأول . فجوابه : من وجهين : الأول : إن هذه القراءة مهجورة مردودة ، وأنها جارية مجرى تغيير القرآن على سبيل التصحيف والتحريف . وهي طريقة مذمومة . الثاني : إن القراءة التي تمسكنا بها ، لا يمكن الطعن فيها . فإنها متواترة . تدل على قولنا . وإذا ثبت ذلك فنقول : هذه القراءة الشاذة . إن كانت منافية لتلك القراءة المتواترة ، وجب القطع بكونها مردودة ، وإن لم تكن منافية لها ، فهي لا تضرنا في هذا المطلوب . وأما الثاني فجوابه : إنا إذا قلنا في الشيء المعين : إنه من فلان . فقد يراد منه : أنه بتخليقه وتكوينه . وقد يراد به : أنه هو الذي أقدره عليه وأعانه عليه وأزال الموانع العائقة منه ، وحصل الشرائط المعتبرة فيه . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : أما كون السيئة من العبد ، فليس المراد أنها حصلت بإقدار العبد . وذلك لأن القدرة على المعصية ، ما حصلت من العبد البتة . ولما تعذر حمل اللفظ على هذا ، وجب حمله على أن المراد منه : أنها حصلت بإيجاده وتكوينه . وأما كون الحسنة من الله ، فهو يحتمل أن يكون المراد
And it is not said: This is refuted from two aspects: The first: That some read 'fa-min nafsik' (from yourself) [as an interrogative]? The second: That Allah attributed 'hasanah' to Himself where He said: 'Whatever hasanah befalls you is from Allah,' and according to the Mu'tazilites, all acts of obedience and disobedience are from the servants. - But we say: As for the first, its answer is from two aspects: The first: That this reading is abandoned and rejected, and it is in the category of changing the Quran by way of distortion and alteration. And it is a blameworthy method. The second: That the reading we adhere to cannot be impugned, for it is mutawatir. It indicates our position. And when that is established, we say: This irregular reading, if it contradicts that mutawatir reading, it must be definitively rejected, and if it does not contradict it, it does not harm us in this matter. As for the second, its answer: When we say of a specific thing 'it is from so-and-so,' it may mean by his creation and bringing into existence, and it may mean that he empowered him for it, aided him in it, removed obstacles from him, and realized the conditions considered in it. When this is established, we say: As for the 'sayyi'ah' being from the servant, it is not intended that it occurred by the servant's empowerment, because the ability (qudra) to commit sin has never been obtained from the servant. And since it is impossible to construe the word in this way, it must be construed to mean that it occurred by his bringing into existence and creation. As for the 'hasanah' being from Allah, it is possible that what is meant is...
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection has two aspects: first, that some read the verse as an interrogative ('from yourself?') implying that it is not from yourself; second, that the Mu'tazilites claim all acts, both obedience and disobedience, come from the servants, but the verse attributes 'hasanah' to Allah, so if 'hasanah' means an act of obedience, it would contradict their view. The author responds to the first objection with two points: first, the irregular reading is rejected as a distortion; second, the mutawatir reading is sound and supports his position. If the irregular reading contradicts the mutawatir, it is rejected; if not, it does not affect the argument. For the second objection, the author explains that when something is attributed to someone, it can mean either that they created it or that they enabled it. For 'sayyi'ah' being from the servant, it cannot mean enabled because the servant does not have the power to commit sin (according to Ash'arite doctrine, all power is from Allah). Therefore, it must mean that the servant creates and brings it into existence. For 'hasanah' from Allah, it could mean either creation or enabling, but the text breaks off before completing the explanation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An irregular Quranic reading can be used to challenge established theological doctrine.
Correction: Irregular readings are rejected as distortions; only mutawatir readings are authoritative.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that some read the verse as 'from yourself?' (interrogative)?
A) He admits it is a possible reading but prefers his own.
B) He argues that the reading is irregular and rejected, and the mutawatir reading is authoritative.
C) He says the reading is not relevant to the argument.
D) He changes his interpretation to accommodate the reading.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'arite position on divine causation is defended by dismissing irregular readings and clarifying that attribution can mean creation or enablement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse that seems to challenge your understanding of divine justice, do you immediately trust the mutawatir readings or do you let isolated reports shake your faith? How can you train yourself to rely on the established transmission?
?
Consider a recent sin you committed. Do you truly believe that Allah created that act, or do you harbor some sense of independent power? How does the distinction between creation and enablement change how you view your own responsibility?
?
If every good deed is from Allah, yet you often take credit for your successes, what does this reveal about your reliance on Him? What concrete step will you take this week to consciously attribute your achievements to Allah alone?
