The Zoroastrian's Argument: Exposing Qadari Logic
A Zoroastrian uses Qadari logic to argue against Amr ibn Ubayd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a Zoroastrian used Qadari reasoning to defeat Amr ibn Ubayd, and why this anecdote refutes the idea of competing wills.

Core Idea
Competing wills lead to dualism; the stronger always wins.

This story from al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents a powerful anecdote used by Sunni scholars to expose the logical consequences of Qadari theology. Amr ibn Ubayd, a leading Mu'tazili, is confronted by a Zoroastrian who turns his own reasoning against him. The passage shows how attributing effective power to creatures alongside God leads to the absurd conclusion that the devil is stronger than God.

Guided Path
1
Read the story
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Pay attention to the dialogue between Amr and the Zoroastrian.
The story is short but dense.
2
Identify the first argument
The Zoroastrian initially says he does not convert because Allah did not will his Islam. This is a Qadari argument: human will is effective and God's will is irrelevant.
3
Amr's response
Amr tries to counter by saying Allah does will his Islam but the devils prevent it. This introduces a second will (the devils) that opposes God's will.
Amr here deviates from pure tawhid by granting the devils independent power.
4
The Zoroastrian's counter
The Zoroastrian points out that the devils' will succeeded while God's did not, so the devils are stronger. He concludes it is better to follow the dominant power.
This exposes the logical consequence: if creatures can effectively oppose God's will, then God is not all-powerful.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this anecdote refutes any theology that grants creatures independent power to override God's will. It is a reductio ad absurdum of Qadari thought.
The story is often used to show that Qadari logic leads to dualism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكاية السادسة : قيل لعمرو بن عبيد : هل الزمك أحد سؤالاً لم تقدر على جوابه ؟ قال : بلى . ركبت البحر ، وكان في السفينة مجوسي . فقلت له : لم لا تسلم ؟ فقال : لأن الله لم يرد إسلامي . فإذا أراد إسلامي أسلمت . فقلت للمجوسي : إن الله يريد إسلامك ، ولكن الشياطين لا يتركونك . فقال المجوسي : فأنا أكون مع الشريك الأغلب . لأن الله أراد شيئاً ، ولم يحصل مراده. والشيطان أرادوه ، وحصل . فالشيطان غالب . والكون مع الغالب ، أولى من الكون مع المغلوب .
The sixth story: It was said to Amr ibn Ubayd: Did anyone force you with a question you could not answer? He said: Yes. I traveled by sea, and there was a Zoroastrian on the ship. I said to him: Why do you not become Muslim? He said: Because Allah did not will my Islam. If He wills my Islam, I will become Muslim. I said to the Zoroastrian: Allah wills your Islam, but the devils do not leave you. The Zoroastrian said: Then I am with the stronger partner. Because Allah willed something and His will did not occur. The devils willed it and it occurred. So the devils are dominant. Being with the dominant is better than being with the dominated.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage recounts how Amr ibn Ubayd, a Mu'tazili, was defeated in argument by a Zoroastrian. The Zoroastrian initially uses the Qadari argument that he does not embrace Islam because God has not willed it. Amr tries to answer by saying that God does will his Islam but the devils prevent him, thus attributing the failure to an obstacle external to God's will. The Zoroastrian then turns the argument back: if God and the devils are two agents with opposing wills, then the one whose will actually occurs is the stronger. Since the devils' will succeeds (the man remains non-Muslim) while God's will fails, the devils are stronger. The Zoroastrian concludes that it is rational to follow the dominant power. This shows that the Qadari position (effective human will) when combined with dual-agency leads to an absurd conclusion that undermines monotheism. The anecdote guards against any theology that makes God's will ineffective or subject to competing wills.

Key Terms
مجوسي
إرادة
شيطان
غالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What conclusion does the Zoroastrian draw from Amr's explanation about the devils?
A) God is weaker than the devils
B) He should follow the stronger partner, which is the devils
C) He will become Muslim later
D) Amr is a Zoroastrian

Key Takeaway
The story demonstrates that any theology granting creatures independent power undermines monotheism and leads to dualism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make excuses for your sins by blaming circumstances or others (like 'the devil made me do it'), are you not effectively saying that something other than Allah's will controls your actions? How does that affect your sincerity in repentance and your submission to Allah's exclusive will?
?
The Zoroastrian says 'I am with the stronger partner.' What 'stronger partner' do you instinctively side with when your desires conflict with Allah's commands? How can you train yourself to side with Allah even when His will seems weaker in the moment?
?
Amr ibn Ubayd was a respected scholar, yet he fell into a logical trap because he compromised on Allah's exclusive will. Is there an area in your faith where you are compromising to fit societal norms? What would it cost you to fully submit to Allah's will in that area?

Saheb ibn Abbad's Sneeze: A Refutation of Independent Will
Saheb ibn Abbad asserts his free will then a sneeze disrupts it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an involuntary sneeze refutes the claim that humans independently create their own actions.

Core Idea
A sneeze proves that not all actions are under our control.

This story from al-Isfarayini uses a real-life incident to challenge the Mu'tazili doctrine of human free will. Saheb ibn Abbad, a prominent figure, insists that every part of his eating is by his choice. But an accidental sneeze and a cat's action show otherwise. Let's see how the narrative makes its theological point.

Guided Path
1
Read the story carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the sequence: assertion, intentional actions, interruption, and outcome.
Pay attention to the repeated phrase 'bi-ikhtiyārī' (by my choice).
2
Identify Saheb's claim
What does Saheb assert about his actions? He says 'the doer of my actions is I' and lists each step as being by his choice.
This is the Mu'tazili position of independent human agency.
3
Analyze the interruption
The sneeze is involuntary—Saheb did not choose it. How does this contradict his previous claim? What does the cat's action add to the lesson?
The unintended fall and the cat's independent action show that not all events are under human control.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكاية السابعة : كان الصاحب بن عباد يؤاكل الأستاذ أبا إسحاق الإسفرايني ، من أصحابنا - فقال الصاحب : لا شك أن فاعل أفعالي أنا لا غيري . ورفع لقمة ووضعها في فمه. وقال : الرفع باختياري ، والوضع في الفم باختياري ، والمضغ باختياري ، والابتلاع باختياري . ثم اتفق في أثناء المضغ أن عطس ، فسقطت اللقمة ، ووثبت الهرة ، فأخذت اللقمة ، وأكلتها
The seventh story: al-Saheb ibn Abbad was eating with the master Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, one of our companions. Al-Saheb said: There is no doubt that the doer of my actions is I, none other. He raised a morsel and put it in his mouth. He said: The raising is by my choice, the placing in the mouth is by my choice, the chewing is by my choice, the swallowing is by my choice. Then during the chewing, he sneezed, so the morsel fell, and the cat jumped, took the morsel, and ate it.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This anecdote illustrates a practical refutation of the claim that humans are entirely independent agents. Saheb ibn Abbad, a Mu'tazili, explicitly asserts that every part of his eating action is by his own choice (bi-ikhtiyārī). However, an involuntary sneeze causes the morsel to fall, and a cat takes it. The sequence shows that despite his claim of complete control, an unforeseen sneeze overrides his will and results in an action (the drop) that he did not intend. The implied argument is that if humans truly created their own actions, such unintended events could not occur. This supports the Ash'ari position that all actions are ultimately under God's control, and human 'acquisition' (kasb) is not independent creation.

Key Terms
فاعل
اختيار
عطس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The story is merely about a random accident with no deeper meaning.
Correction: It is a deliberate theological demonstration that human will is subject to external factors beyond one's control, challenging the Mu'tazili view of independent human agency.

Checkpoint
What event disproves Saheb ibn Abbad's claim of complete personal choice?
A) He choked on the morsel
B) He sneezed and the morsel fell
C) The cat refused to eat
D) Abu Ishaq disagreed with him

Key Takeaway
An involuntary sneeze and its consequences show that humans do not have complete independent control over their actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment today when you felt completely in control of your actions. Now realize that your next breath, your heartbeat, and even the urge to move are not ultimately from you. How would acknowledging this change the way you begin each action?
?
When have you made a plan that was shattered by something as small as a sneeze? How did that remind you of your dependence on God? Will you now consciously dedicate every plan to Him before starting?
?
Do you ever catch yourself saying 'I did this' or 'I chose that' without remembering that God is the true Creator of your actions? What one habit can you adopt to constantly attribute your actions to His power?

Saheb and the Fruit: Argument from Opposite Actions
Abu Ishaq argues that if one can separate a fruit, one should also be able to reattach it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument from opposite actions used by Ash'ari scholars to refute the Mu'tazili concept of independent human power.

Core Idea
If you can separate a fruit but cannot reattach it, your power is not absolute.

This lesson presents a vivid anecdote from Ash'ari–Mu'tazili debate. Abu Ishaq uses a simple act—picking a fruit—to expose a contradiction in the Mu'tazili definition of power. By the end, you will see how the inability to reverse an action reveals that human power is limited and acquired from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the story and the key terms.
Pay attention to the words فصل (fāṣala) and وصل (waṣala) – separation and reattachment.
2
Understand the Mu'tazili definition of power
The passage explains that the Mu'tazila define qudra (power) as the ability to perform both an action and its opposite (ḍiddayn). Grasp this definition.
This definition is central to the argument.
3
Follow the logical consequence
If al-Saheb had the power to separate the fruit, he must also have the power to reattach it (by the Mu'tazili definition). Since he cannot reattach, he does not truly have power over the separation.
This is a reductio ad absurdum of the Mu'tazili view.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider what this argument tells us about human power. It is limited and not independent; true power to create belongs to Allah alone.
This refutes the Mu'tazili claim of human free will and creation of actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكاية الثامنة : وحكى أن الصاحب بن عباد كان جالساً مع الأستاذ أبي اسحاق ، في بستان ، فمد يده إلى ثمرة وفصلها عن الشجرة . وقال : أيها الأستاذ من الذي فصل هذه الثمرة عن الشجرة ؟ فقال الأستاذ أبي اسحاق : الذي فصل هذه الثمرة عن هذا الغصن ، هو الذي يقدر على أن يصلها به مرة أخرى. والمراد منه : أن القادر عند المعتزلة لا بد وأن يكون قادراً على الضدين . فلو كان العبد قادراً على الفصل ، لوجب أن يكون قادراً على الوصل . وحيث لم يقدر على الوصل ، وجب أن لا يقدر على الفصل .
The eighth story: It is related that al-Saheb ibn Abbad was sitting with the master Abu Ishaq in a garden. He reached his hand to a fruit and separated it from the tree. He said: O master, who separated this fruit from the tree? The master Abu Ishaq said: He who separated this fruit from this branch is the one who is able to reattach it to it again. The intended meaning is that the agent (qādir) according to the Mu'tazila must be capable of opposites (ḍiddayn). So if the servant is capable of separation, he must be capable of reattachment. But since he is not able to reattach, it is necessary that he is not able to separate.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This anecdote presents a logical argument against the Mu'tazili concept of human power (qudra). Al-Saheb, a Mu'tazili, picks a fruit and asks who did it, expecting the answer 'I did.' But Abu Ishaq, the Ash'ari scholar, responds that the same agent must be capable of the opposite action (reattaching) if he is truly powerful (qādir). The Mu'tazila define power as the ability to perform both an action and its opposite (qudra ʿalā al-ḍiddayn). Abu Ishaq applies this definition to the fruit separation: if al-Saheb truly had the power to separate, he should also have the power to reattach. Since he cannot reattach, he does not genuinely have power over the initial separation. The point is that human power is limited and not creative; it is acquired from Allah. This refutes the Mu'tazili claim that humans independently create their actions.

Key Terms
قادر
ضد
فصل
وصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that humans perform any actions; they are passive.
Correction: The argument does not deny that humans perform actions; it denies that they have independent creative power. Human power is acquired from Allah and is limited.

Checkpoint
What logical consequence does Abu Ishaq derive from the Mu'tazili definition of power (qudra)?
A) Humans can also create fruits
B) The ability to separate implies ability to reattach
C) Fruits do not exist
D) Power is not necessary for actions

Key Takeaway
The inability to reverse an action shows that human power is not independent but acquired from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you feel it is entirely your own achievement, or do you sense that your ability to do it is a gift from Allah? How would acknowledging your limited power change the way you feel about your successes?
?
Think of a sin you committed that you cannot undo. In what ways does this lesson show you that your own power is insufficient to reverse its consequences, and how does that drive you to rely on Allah's mercy and forgiveness?
?
Abu Ishaq used a simple fruit to expose a profound truth about human dependence. Look at the last action you performed—any action—and ask yourself: if you cannot fully control its outcome or undo it, who truly is the agent? What would it mean for you to live each moment in awareness that your power is always borrowed?

Two Statements, Two Theologies
Anecdote presenting two doctrinal statements in dialogue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two brief phrases from Abd al-Jabbar and Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini encapsulate the core divergence between Mu'tazili and Ash'ari theology on divine justice and sovereignty.

Core Idea
Two sentences, two theologies: one emphasizes God's transcendence from evil, the other His absolute sovereignty.

This short anecdote places two great theological minds in a single room. Their spontaneous exchanges—a mere sentence each—reveal the entire framework of their respective schools. As we examine these words, you will see how a Mu'tazili and an Ash'ari each compressed their entire system into one declaration about God.

Guided Path
1
Read the story
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the setting: a master and a qadi meet, and each responds instantly.
Pay attention to the phrase 'في الحال'—their responses were immediate, showing how central these ideas were.
2
Identify the two statements
Find Abd al-Jabbar's statement: 'سبحان من تنزه عن الفحشاء' and Abu Ishaq's response: 'سبحان من لا يجري في ملكه إلا ما يشاء'. Note the contrast.
The first emphasizes what God is free from; the second emphasizes what God has control over.
3
Unpack the theological implications
Reflect on why Abd al-Jabbar's words reflect Mu'tazili emphasis on divine justice (God must be free from evil), and Abu Ishaq's words reflect Ash'ari emphasis on divine will (everything occurs by God's will).
Consider how each statement implies different understandings of good, evil, and human responsibility.
4
Consider the commentator's insight
Read Muhammad al-Razi's comment: each speaker gathered all his words in this point. This means each sentence is a summary of a whole doctrinal system.
This guards against thinking these are just casual remarks; they are creedal summaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكاية التاسعة : كان الأستاذ أبو اسحاق الإسفرايني جالساً في دار الصاحب بن عباد ، فدخل القاضي عبد الجبار الهمداني ، فلما رأى الأستاذ أبا إسحاق ، قال في الحال : سبحان من تنزه عن الفحشاء . فقال الأستاذ في الحال: سبحان من لا يجري في ملكه إلا ما يشاء . قال الداعي إلى الله ، محمد الرازي - رحمة الله عليه - : من نظر في هاتين الكلمتين ، علم أن كل واحد من هذين جمع جميع كلماته في هذا الحرف
The ninth story: The master Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini was sitting in the house of al-Saheb ibn Abbad, when al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar al-Hamadhani entered. When he saw the master Abu Ishaq, he immediately said: 'Glorified is He who is far removed from indecency.' The master immediately replied: 'Glorified is He who does not allow to occur in His dominion except what He wills.' The caller to Allah, Muhammad al-Razi — may Allah's mercy be upon him — said: Whoever looks at these two statements knows that each of them gathered all his words in this point.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This anecdote captures the essence of the theological conflict between Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna in two concise statements. When Abd al-Jabbar (a Mu'tazili) enters, he declares Allah's transcendence (tanzīh) from indecency (fahshā'), reflecting the Mu'tazili emphasis on divine justice, which requires that God not create evil. Abu Ishaq (an Ash'ari) responds with Allah's absolute sovereignty: nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills, affirming that all events, including evil, are by Allah's will. The commentator Muhammad al-Razi notes that each statement encapsulates the speaker's entire doctrinal system. This guards against the idea that God's will is limited by human notions of good and evil.

Key Terms
سبحان
تنزه
فحشاء
ملك
مشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That both statements say the same thing about God's transcendence.
Correction: They express opposing theological emphases: one stresses God's freedom from evil (Mu'tazili), the other stresses God's absolute control (Ash'ari).

Checkpoint
What did Abd al-Jabbar's statement 'سبحان من تنزه عن الفحشاء' emphasize?
A) God's sovereignty over all events
B) God's freedom from indecency
C) God's mercy
D) God's oneness

Key Takeaway
A brief anecdote shows that the entire Mu'tazili and Ash'ari positions on divine justice and will can be captured in two opposed declarations about God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you utter phrases like 'Subhan Allah' in daily conversation, do you consider whether they reflect a specific theological stance? What does your own use of such phrases reveal about your understanding of God's attributes? Pause and ask yourself: Are your words mere habits, or do they align with what you truly believe about Allah's justice and will?
?
Imagine you were in the gathering described—would you have recognized the depth behind Abd al-Jabbar's and Abu Ishaq's statements, or would you have heard only surface-level praises? How often do you listen to religious speech with discernment, seeking the underlying creed? Commit today to paying closer attention to the theological implications of the words you hear and say.
?
The two scholars distilled their entire theologies into single sentences. If you were forced to summarize your own beliefs about Allah's justice and will in one brief declaration, what would you say? Would it reflect the Mu'tazili position, the Ash'ari position, or something else? Write down your own 'creed in a sentence' and compare it to the sources. Is it consistent with what you learn from the Quran and Sunnah?

Responding to the Adhan with 'La Hawla'
The response to 'Hayya ala al-salah' is explained as affirming God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sunna response to 'Hayya ʿalā al-ṣalāh' in the adhan and its theological significance affirming divine assistance for all worship.

Core Idea
The phrase 'La hawla' during adhan affirms that all power for worship comes from Allah alone.

The adhan calls believers to prayer, and the prescribed response to the phrases 'Hayya ʿalā al-ṣalāh' and 'Hayya ʿalā al-falāḥ' is not just a passive listening but an active affirmation of divine power. This lesson examines the juristic consensus on this sunna and the deep theological point it conveys about human dependence on Allah's help.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully and its English translation. Notice the context: it is the tenth anecdote in a chapter.
The passage is short but dense with meaning.
2
Identify the adhan phrase that triggers the response
The passage specifies that when the caller says 'Hayya ʿalā al-ṣalāh' and 'Hayya ʿalā al-falāḥ', the sunna is to say 'Lā ḥawla wa lā quwwata illā billāh'. Note that this response is specific to these phrases, not to other parts of the adhan.
3
Understand the interpretation of 'La hawla'
The passage explains that the phrase is as if saying there is no change or power to perform prayer or any worship except by Allah's help and success. This interpretation ties the response directly to divine assistance.
The words 'ḥawl' and 'quwwa' refer to power and strength.
4
Recognize the doctrinal assertion
The passage explicitly states 'This is the explicit doctrine of our school' (Ash'ari). This shows that the response is not just a practice but a point of theology affirming that human actions depend on God's power.
This guards against any belief in independent human power.
5
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider why this anecdote is placed at the end of the chapter. It serves as a concluding summary of the Ash'ari perspective on divine will and human action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكاية العاشرة : قال أكثر الفقهاء : إذا قال المؤذن : حي على الصلاة، حي على الفلاح. فالسنّة عند سماع هذه الكلمة : أن يقال : لا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله . كأنه يقول : لا حول ولا قوة على أداء الصلاة ، وعلى أداء سائر العبادات، إلا بإعانة الله وبتوفيقه . وذلك صريح مذهبنا . ولنكتف بهذه الحكايات في هذا الباب . والله أعلم بالصواب
The tenth story: Most jurists said: When the caller to prayer says 'Hayya ʿalā al-ṣalāh, Hayya ʿalā al-falāḥ,' it is sunna upon hearing this phrase to say: 'Lā ḥawla wa lā quwwata illā billāh.' As if he says: There is no change and no power for performing prayer and for performing all other acts of worship except by Allah's help and His success. And that is explicit from our school. And we suffice with these stories in this chapter. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This final anecdote serves as a conclusion to the chapter, providing a concise doctrinal summary from the Ash'ari perspective. The majority of jurists (fuqahā') state that upon hearing the call to prayer, one should say 'Lā ḥawla wa lā quwwata illā billāh.' This phrase is interpreted to mean that no ability or power to perform prayer or any worship exists except through Allah's assistance and grace (tawfīq). This is presented as the explicit doctrine (madhhab) of the Ash'aris: all human actions depend on God's power and will. The narrator concludes by stating that these stories suffice for this chapter. The passage guards against any doctrine that attributes independent power to humans.

Key Terms
حول
قوة
إعانة
توفيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that saying 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' negates human responsibility or suggests that humans are mere puppets.
Correction: The phrase does not negate human responsibility but affirms that all ability to act is granted by Allah. Humans still choose and act, but the power to do so comes from Allah's assistance and grace.

Checkpoint

A) Humans have no responsibility
B) All ability for worship comes from Allah's help
C) Prayer is impossible
D) The adhan is invalid

Key Takeaway
The sunna response to the adhan's call to prayer is to affirm that all power for worship comes from Allah's help alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the adhan and respond with 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah', do you truly feel your utter dependence on Allah for the ability to pray, or is it just words? What would change if you paused to reflect on its meaning before rushing to prayer?
?
The passage teaches that all acts of worship are only possible through Allah's help. Is there an act of obedience you've been putting off because you think you can't do it? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
If you were to truly internalize that without Allah's tawfiq you cannot even perform a prayer, how would that affect your attitude towards your daily worship? Would it lead you to more humility before each prayer, or more urgency in seeking Allah's help?

The Four Main Arguments of the Mu'tazila on Human Agency
The author summarizes the four main arguments of the Mu'tazila against divine causation of human acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four main arguments (شبهات) that the Mu'tazila used to argue for human agency against the Ash'ari position of divine causation.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's case for human agency rests on four key arguments: experience, reason, and theology.

The Mu'tazila were known for their strong emphasis on human free will and rational justice. Al-Matalib al-Aliyah summarizes their four main arguments against the Ash'ari view that God creates all human actions. These arguments draw on self-evident experience, logical consistency, and the very foundations of Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Know that this passage summarizes the Mu'tazili position, not the author's own view. The author is presenting their arguments to refute them later.
2
Read the first argument
The first argument states that our actions correlate with our capacity and will, so they must be caused by these faculties.
This is a causal argument based on observation.
3
Read the second argument
The second argument appeals to our self-evident sense of ability to choose between action and inaction.
This is an argument from immediate experience (wijdān).
4
Read the third argument
The third argument warns that denying human agency leads to impossible obligations and attributing evil to God, which reason deems repugnant.
This is a theological and ethical argument.
5
Read the fourth argument
The fourth argument claims that without human agency, the proofs for God, prophethood, and the Qur'an's miracle become impossible.
This is a foundational argument for Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا مجموع كلام القوم في هذا الباب
واعلم : أن هذه الوجوه - وإن كثرت - إلا أن حاصلها راجع إلى شبهات أربعة :
أولها : إن وجود أفعالنا دائر مع حصول قصورنا وإرادتنا ، فوجب أن يكون وقوع هذه الأفعال بها
وثانيها : إن المكنة من الفعل والترك ، أمر وجداني اضطراري ، وكون الله تعالى موجداً لأفعال العباد ، يبطل هذا الوجدان
وثالثها : إن العبد لو لم يكن موجداً لأفعال نفسه ، يلزم أن يكون تكليفه جارياً مجرى تكليف العاجز . وذلك قبيح في العقل . ولزم كونه تعالى خالقاً لهذه القبائح ، وبشتم نفسه . وكل ذلك قبيح في العقول .
ورابعها : إنه لو لم يكن العبد موجدا لأفعال نفسه ، لزم العجز عن إثبات الإله ، وعن إثبات النبوة ، وعن كون القرآن معجزاً . وكل ذلك باطل .
This is the totality of the people's speech in this chapter. Know that these arguments - although numerous - yet their essence reduces to four doubts: First: That the existence of our actions revolves around the occurrence of our capacity and will, so it is necessary that the occurrence of these actions be by them. Second: That the ability to do or refrain is a necessary self-evident matter, and that Allah, exalted, being the creator of the slaves' actions invalidates this self-evident perception. Third: That if the slave is not the creator of his own actions, then his obligation would be like the obligation of the incapable. That is repugnant in reason. And it would necessitate that He, exalted, be the creator of these ugly acts, and insult Himself. All of that is repugnant in intellects. Fourth: That if the slave is not the creator of his own actions, it would necessitate inability to prove God, to prove prophethood, and to prove that the Qur'an is a miracle. All of that is false.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the four main arguments (شبهات) that the Mu'tazila use against the Ash'ari position that God creates all actions, including human actions. The author notes that despite the many arguments presented earlier, they all reduce to these four. First argument: Our actions correlate with our capacity (qudra) and will (irāda), so they must be caused by these. Second argument: We have a self-evident (wijdānī) sense of ability to do or not do, so saying God creates our actions contradicts this immediate experience. Third argument: Denying that servants create their actions leads to obligations beyond capacity (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq), which is rationally evil (qabīḥ), and would make God the creator of evil acts and insulting Himself, all rationally abhorrent. Fourth argument: Without human agency, proofs for God's existence, prophethood, and the Qur'an's inimitability (iʿjāz) collapse, leading to falsehood.

Key Terms
شبهات
أفعال العباد
قدرة
إرادة
تكليف
قبيح
وجدان
عجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These arguments represent the author's own Ash'ari position.
Correction: These are actually the Mu'tazila's arguments that the author is summarizing in order to refute them.

Checkpoint
Which of the four arguments claims that denying human agency makes it impossible to prove God's existence?
A) The first argument based on correlation of action with will and capacity
B) The second argument based on self-evident ability
C) The third argument based on obligation of the incapable
D) The fourth argument based on inability to prove God, prophethood, and the Qur'an's miracle

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's four arguments against divine causation are based on causation, immediate experience, rational ethics, and theological foundations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you truly feel it is your own choice, or do you sometimes excuse yourself by saying 'it was written'? How does that affect your repentance and resolve to change?
?
If you believed that every action you do is directly created by God—including your disobedience—how would that change your understanding of His justice and your personal responsibility? Would you still strive to obey?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that without human agency, the proof of God collapses. Reflect: does your certainty in God depend on your sense of free will? If you lost that sense, would your faith remain intact?

Introduction to the Mu'tazila's Rational Proofs
Author introduces chapter and states method of conciseness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the author introduces the chapter on the Mu'tazila's rational proofs and explains his method of concisely summarizing their main points.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila have many arguments; the author will condense them to their essentials.

This brief passage opens Chapter Six, setting the stage for the author's presentation of the Mu'tazila's rational proofs. The author acknowledges the vastness of their discourse and declares his intent to focus only on their core arguments. This approach allows readers to grasp the key points without getting lost in extensive details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

The phrase 'اعلم أن' signals an important declaration.
2

Introduction to Mu'tazila Logical Proofs
Ibn al-Qayyim introduces the sixth chapter on Mu'tazila arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the purpose and approach of Ibn al-Qayyim's sixth chapter on Mu'tazila arguments.

Core Idea
Ibn al-Qayyim sets the stage for a concise summary of Mu'tazila logical proofs.

The sixth chapter of Ibn al-Qayyim's work turns to the arguments of the Mu'tazila sect. Before diving into details, the author makes a brief announcement: the Mu'tazila have produced a massive body of discourse, so he will not attempt to cover everything. Instead, he will focus on their key points in a condensed manner. This short opening passage sets the reader's expectations and frames the chapter as a summary of core arguments rather than an exhaustive treatment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'على سبيل الاختصار' which signals summarization.
2
Identify the chapter's focus
The passage tells us the chapter is about 'الدلائل القوانية' (logical proofs) that the Mu'tazila adhere to. Ask yourself: what does this tell you about the content to come?
3
Note the author's stated approach
Ibn al-Qayyim says he will only 'point to the knots of their statements' in summary. This means he will not go into full detail. Reflect on why he might choose summarization over exhaustive coverage.
This helps you understand the scope and limitation of the chapter.
4
Consider the implied audience
The passage assumes the reader is familiar with Mu'tazila discourse being 'lengthy and abundant.' Think about what kind of reader Ibn al-Qayyim is addressing.
This sets expectations for the depth of treatment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السادس
حكاية الدلائل القوانية التي يتمسكون بها
اعلم : أن كلام المعتزلة في هذا الباب قد طال وكثر . ونحن نشير إلى
معاقد أقوالهم على سبيل الاختصار
The sixth chapter: Narrating the logical proofs that they adhere to. Know: that the speech of the Mu'tazila in this chapter has become lengthy and abundant. And we will point to the knots of their statements in a summary manner.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is an introductory notice for the sixth chapter of the book. The author states the chapter's topic: narrating the 'logical proofs' (al-dala'il al-qawaniyya) that the Mu'tazila adhere to. He then notes that the Mu'tazila's discourse on this subject is extensive and very lengthy. Therefore, he will not cover it in full detail but rather will point to the central points (ma'aqid) of their statements in a summarized manner. The passage sets the reader's expectation for a condensed presentation of Mu'tazila arguments.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الدلائل القوانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The chapter will provide a detailed exhaustive treatment of Mu'tazila arguments.
Correction: Ibn al-Qayyim explicitly states he will only summarize the central points, not present every detail.

Checkpoint
What approach does Ibn al-Qayyim announce for presenting the Mu'tazila arguments in this chapter?
A) A detailed and exhaustive treatment
B) A summary focusing on the central points
C) A refutation without presentation
D) A verbatim quotation of all their works

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Qayyim begins the chapter by clarifying that he will present only the core Mu'tazila logical proofs in a condensed form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly take time to understand the central points of an opposing view before responding, just as Ibn al-Qayyim does here? In your next disagreement, will you commit to summarizing their argument first?
?
When faced with a lengthy discourse from someone you consider misguided, do you dismiss it or do you humbly seek to grasp its essence? This lesson calls you to a scholarly humility. Will you adopt it?

First Argument: Seeking Refuge from a Powerless Satan is Absurd
The author presents the first of six arguments refuting the Mu'tazilite position on isti'adha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the act of seeking refuge (isti'adha) from Satan leads to a rational refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that Allah has no part in evil.

Core Idea
If Allah has no hand in Satan's evil, then seeking refuge from Him is either futile or impossible.

This lesson introduces the first of six rational arguments against the Mu'tazilite position that Allah does not create evil deeds. The argument draws directly from the Quranic command to seek refuge in Allah from Satan. By analyzing the logical implications of this act, the author exposes a contradiction in the Mu'tazilite view. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall argument. Notice the concise style and logical structure.
The author uses a disjunction (either...or) as the main argumentative move.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The argument presents two possible meanings of 'seeking refuge from Satan': either Allah prevents Satan by rebuke/threat, or by compulsion/coercion. Identify each in the text.
These two options exhaust the logical possibilities on the Mu'tazilite assumption.
3
Analyze why each option fails
First option: Allah has already rebuked and warned, so requesting it is asking for what is already done (futile). Second option: compulsion would negate Satan's moral accountability, which is impossible for a just God. Understand why each leads to a contradiction.
The argument does not assume which option is correct; it shows both are problematic.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since both possibilities are invalid, the Mu'tazilite premise—that Allah has no effect on Satan's evil—must be false. This shows that the Quranic command to seek refuge logically requires Allah's involvement in Satan's actions.
This argument does not endorse Jabr; it refutes the Mu'tazilite position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(أعوذ بالله من الشيطان الرجيم) توجب فساد الجبر، فههنا وجوه أخرى مستنبطة منها توجب فساد القول بالاعتزال. فالأول: إن شر الشياطين والأبالسة إذا لم يكن الله تعالى فيه أثر البتة، لم تجز الاستعاذة بالله. لأن على هذا التقدير إما أن يكون المطلوب من الله في هذه الاستعاذة: أن يمنع الشيطان بالنبي والزجر والتهديد، أو بأن يمنعه منه بالقهر والإلجاء. أما الأول فقد فعله الله تعالى، فكان طلبه لتحصيل الحاصل، وهو عبث. وإن كان الثاني لم يجز من الله تعالى أن يفعله، لأن الإلجاء ينافي كون الشيطان مكلفاً.
(I seek refuge in Allah from the accursed Satan) necessitates the corruption of Jabr. Here there are other derived aspects that necessitate the corruption of the Mu'tazilite position. The first: If the evil of the devils and satans has absolutely no effect from Allah Almighty, then seeking refuge in Allah is not permissible. Because on this assumption, what is requested from Allah in this seeking refuge is either that He prevents the Satan by rebuke, reprimand, and threat, or that He prevents him from him by compulsion and coercion. As for the first, Allah Almighty has already done it, so requesting it would be requesting what is already obtained, which is futile. And if it is the second, it is not permissible for Allah Almighty to do it, because coercion contradicts the Satan being accountable.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage presents the first of six rational arguments against the Mu'tazilite position that evil deeds are not created by Allah. The author begins by noting that the very act of seeking refuge (isti'adha) from Satan, as commanded in the Quran, undermines the Mu'tazilite view. The first argument proceeds as follows: If Allah has no part whatsoever in the evil of Satan, then seeking refuge in Allah from Satan is meaningless. Why? Because the request in isti'adha can only be one of two things: either (a) that Allah prevent Satan by rebuke or threat (which is already done, as Allah has already warned humanity), so asking for it is futile; or (b) that Allah prevent Satan by compulsion (ilja'), which would force Satan against his free will and contradict his being a morally accountable being (mukallaf). Thus neither option is valid, proving that the Mu'tazilite premise—that Allah has no hand in Satan's evil—is false.

Key Terms
الاستعاذة
الجبر
الاعتزال
الشيطان
الإلجاء
التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument supports the Jabri position of absolute predestination.
Correction: This argument refutes the Mu'tazilite position by exposing an internal contradiction in their view, not by endorsing Jabr. The author explicitly states the argument also corrupts Jabr.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why is seeking refuge in Allah from Satan futile if Allah has no part in Satan's evil?
A) Because Satan is stronger than Allah.
B) Because either the request is already fulfilled or it requires compulsion, which contradicts Satan's accountability.
C) Because Allah cannot prevent Satan.
D) Because the Prophet intercedes instead.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic command to seek refuge in Allah logically requires that Allah has an effect on Satan's evil, otherwise the act would be futile or impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I seek refuge in Allah from the accursed Satan,' do you truly believe that Allah can and will prevent that evil—or do you say it out of habit, without any real expectation? What would it take for you to mean it with full conviction?
?
The author argues that asking for what Allah has already done is futile. Is your recitation of isti'adha accompanied by a sense of sincere reliance on Allah's active protection, or is it mere ritual? How can you turn it into a genuine plea that changes your state of heart?
?
If you truly accepted that Allah has no part in the evil that tempts or harms you, would you still seek refuge from Him with the same urgency? How does this lesson expose a hidden contradiction in your own reliance on Allah during trials? What will you change in your supplication starting today?

The Dilemma of Allah's Will and Satan's Corruption
The author presents a logical dilemma about Allah's will and Satan's expected corruption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that arises if one denies Allah's effective will over Satan's actions.

Core Idea
If Allah wills reform but creates a corrupter, His actions contradict His will.

This lesson examines a second argument that targets the Mu'tazilite position by exposing a contradiction between Allah's desire for reform and the existence of Satan, who corrupts. The author presents a four-branch dilemma that shows the futility of seeking refuge if Allah's will is not absolute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure: it starts with a disjunction (إما... وإما) and then explores each branch.
The argument is concise; each branch leads to an absurdity.
2
Identify the two main branches
The first branch assumes Allah wills reform; the second assumes He does not. Understand each as a separate path.
Each main branch has further sub-branches.
3
Analyze the sub-branches of the first main branch
Within the first branch, consider whether Satan is expected to corrupt. If yes, why create him? If no, why seek refuge?
Both sub-branches lead to a contradiction or pointlessness.
4
Understand the second main branch
If Allah does not will reform, then seeking refuge cannot provide protection, because Allah's will is not behind it.
This branch also renders isti'adha ineffective.
5
Connect to the broader theological debate
The dilemma shows that without divine will encompassing all actions, the practice of seeking refuge becomes contradictory or futile. This undermines the Mu'tazilite view.
The argument is about the logical consequences of denying Allah's universal will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: إن الله تعالى إما أن يكون مريداً لصلاح حال العبد، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك. فإن كان الأول فالشيطان إما أن يتوقع منه إفساد العبد، أو لا يتوقع منه ذلك. فإن كان المتوقع من الشيطان إفساد العبد، أن الله تعالى يريد إصلاحه، فلم خلقه؟ ولم يسلطه على العبد؟ وإن كان لا يتوقع منه إفساد العبد، فأي فائدة في الاستعاذة بالله منه؟ وأما الثاني: فهو أن الله قد لا يريد إصلاح العبد، وعلى هذا التقدير: الاستعاذة بالله لا تفيد الاعتصام من آفات الدين والدنيا.
The second: Allah Almighty is either willing the reform of the servant's state, or He is not. If the first, then Satan is either expected to corrupt the servant, or not expected that. If corruption is expected from Satan while Allah wants to reform him, then why did He create him? And why did He give him power over the servant? And if corruption is not expected from Satan, then what benefit is there in seeking refuge in Allah from him? As for the second: it is that Allah may not will the reform of the servant, and on this assumption, seeking refuge in Allah does not provide protection from the afflictions of religion and this world.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument. The author sets up a dilemma: either Allah wills the reform (salah) of the servant, or He does not. In the first case, if Allah wills reform, then we consider whether Satan is expected to corrupt the servant. If Satan is expected to corrupt him, then Allah's will to reform contradicts His creating and empowering Satan to corrupt. If Satan is not expected to corrupt, then there is no need to seek refuge from him. In the second case, if Allah does not will reform for the servant, then seeking refuge cannot protect him anyway. Thus, in all scenarios, the Mu'tazilite position makes isti'adha either contradictory or useless.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الصلاح
الفساد
الاستعاذة
الشيطان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this argument claims Allah directly causes evil or that Satan's actions are outside divine will.
Correction: The argument does not make a direct claim about causation; it exposes a logical contradiction in the Mu'tazilite view that Allah wills reform but creates a force that inevitably frustrates that will, without attributing evil to Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, if Allah wills reform for a servant and expects Satan to corrupt him, what is the problem?
A) Satan is too weak to corrupt.
B) It is contradictory for Allah to create and empower a force that frustrates His own will.
C) The servant is already perfect.
D) Seeking refuge is forbidden.

Key Takeaway
The logical dilemma shows that without divine will encompassing all actions, the practice of seeking refuge becomes contradictory or futile.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe Allah wills your reform, why do you still fear the influence of Satan more than you rely on Allah's will? Are you acting as if Allah's will can be defeated?
?
When you seek refuge from Satan, do you do so with the confidence that Allah's will protects you, or do you see it as a mere ritual? What does that reveal about your understanding of divine will?
?
The dilemma shows that if Allah does not will your reform, your seeking refuge is empty. Does your own experience of turning to Allah feel effective? If not, what might that imply about your belief in Allah's will?

The Dilemma of Satan's Will: A Refutation of Mu'tazilism
Author presents a dilemma on Satan's freedom leading to jabr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational dilemma showing that the Mu'tazilite view on free will leads to either compulsion or determinism, both contradicting their own principles.

Core Idea
Satan's choice reveals that free will must be determined by God – a dilemma for Mu'tazilites.

This lesson presents a powerful rational argument against the Mu'tazilite position on free will. The author uses a simple dilemma: either Satan is compelled to do evil, or he has free capacity. In both cases, the conclusion is divine determinism (jabr), which the Mu'tazilites reject. By the end, you will see why this argument is considered a knock-down refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then its translation. Let the dilemma structure become clear.
The passage is short but dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Notice the two possibilities for Satan: either compelled or capable of both good and evil. Trace each horn's consequence.
The first horn directly contradicts Mu'tazilism; the second leads to jabr via the need for a preponderating factor.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand why both horns lead to jabr, and why the author asks the rhetorical question about seeking refuge.
This shows the incoherence of the Mu'tazilite position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: إن الشيطان إما أن يكون مجبورا على فعل الشر، أو لا يكون قادراً على الشر والخير. فإن كان الأول فقد أجبره الله تعالى على الشر. وذلك يناقض الاعتزال. وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ لا تترجح فاعلية الشر على فاعلية الخير، إلا لمرجح من قبل الله تعالى - على ما قررناه مراراً وأطواراً - فحينئذ يلزم الجبر . فكيف يستعاذ من الشيطان؟
The third: Satan is either compelled to do evil, or he is not capable of evil and good. If the first, then Allah has compelled him to evil, and that contradicts Mu'tazilism. If the second, then the actualization of evil is not preponderant over the actualization of good except by a preponderating factor from Allah Almighty – as we have established repeatedly and in various ways – and then jabr (determinism) follows. So how can one seek refuge from Satan?
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument, which is a classic dilemma regarding free will. The author states that Satan is either compelled (majbur) to do evil, or he is not capable of both good and evil (meaning he has free choice). The first option (compulsion) directly contradicts the Mu'tazilite belief in human free will, as it makes Satan's evil forced by Allah. The second option (free capacity) introduces a problem: if Satan has equal ability to do good or evil, an external preponderating factor (murajjih) from Allah must tip the balance towards evil, because a free will cannot arbitrarily choose one without a reason. This again leads to divine determination (jabr). Thus in both cases, the Mu'tazilite position collapses into jabr, making isti'adha incoherent.

Key Terms
الجبر
الترجيح
المرجح
الشر
الخير
الفاعلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans can freely choose without any external cause or preponderating factor.
Correction: The argument shows that without a preponderating factor from Allah, no choice can be made; all actions are ultimately determined by Allah.

Checkpoint
In the third argument, what does the author say about Satan's capacity for evil and good?
A) Satan is compelled to evil, which is acceptable to Mu'tazilites.
B) Satan has equal capacity for good and evil, requiring a divine preponderating factor for evil, which implies jabr.
C) Satan is free to choose both, and no external factor is needed.
D) Satan cannot do evil, only good.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite view on free will leads to either compulsion or determinism, both unacceptable, as shown by the dilemma of Satan.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you seek refuge from Satan (say ‘A‘ūdhu billāh’) without truly believing that Allah is the one who determines whether harm reaches you? Does this dilemma expose a lack of sincerity in your isti‘ādha?
?
If the preponderance of your good deeds over your bad deeds depends entirely on Allah’s mercy, are you more humbly reliant on Him, or do you secretly credit yourself for your choices?
?
When you fail to do good, do you truly accept that whatever Allah wills happens, or do you harbor resentment that He didn't ‘preponderate’ better for you? How can you cultivate contentment with His decree today?

Divine Foreknowledge and the Futility of Seeking Refuge
The author presents a logical argument that divine foreknowledge negates the benefit of seeking refuge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine foreknowledge is used to argue that seeking refuge (isti'adhah) is futile, and how this argument challenges the Mu'tazilite view.

Core Idea
If Allah already knows the outcome, seeking refuge cannot change it.

This lesson unpacks a concise yet powerful argument from al-Matalib al-Aliyah. The author uses the doctrine of divine omniscience to question the utility of seeking refuge from harmful events. By examining the logical dichotomy of known occurrence versus non-occurrence, we see a rational challenge to the Mu'tazilite belief that such acts can alter foreknown outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Notice the conditional structure: if the act is known to occur, then necessary; if known not to occur, then impossible.
This is a classic logical dilemma.
2
Understand the two branches
Identify the two possibilities for the act from which refuge is sought. Understand why each leads to the conclusion that seeking refuge is useless.
The argument assumes Allah's knowledge is infallible and unchangeable.
3
Consider the underlying theological assumptions
Reflect on the implication that divine foreknowledge renders human actions ineffective in changing outcomes. This is a critique of the Mu'tazilite view that human agency can influence what is foreknown.
Muslim orthodoxy holds that Allah's knowledge does not compel human actions but is simply knowledge of what will happen.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Relate this argument to the ongoing sectarian discussion between Ash'aris and Mu'tazilites on divine attributes and human free will.
This argument is one of several refutations by the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: إن كان الفعل المستعاذ منه: معلوم الوقوع. فهو واجب الوقوع. وإن كان معلوم اللاوقوع، فهو ممتنع الوقوع. وعلى التقديرين فلا فائدة في الاستعاذة.
The fourth: If the act from which refuge is sought is known to occur, then its occurrence is necessary. And if it is known not to occur, then its occurrence is impossible. In both cases there is no benefit in seeking refuge.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This very concise fourth argument uses divine omniscience. The act from which one seeks refuge (e.g., Satan's harm) is either known by Allah to occur or known not to occur. If it is known to occur, then it is necessary (wajib) and inevitable—seeking refuge cannot change it. If it is known not to occur, then it is impossible (mumtani')—seeking refuge is redundant. Therefore, on either assumption, the act of seeking refuge serves no purpose. This argument presupposes that Allah's knowledge is infallible and that human actions cannot alter what is eternally known.

Key Terms
العلم
الوقوع
الوجوب
الامتناع
الاستعاذة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies free will and suggests that all actions are predestined regardless of human effort.
Correction: The argument is a logical challenge to the Mu'tazilite view that seeking refuge can alter Allah's eternal knowledge. It does not negate human responsibility; rather, it questions the utility of a specific action under the assumption of unchangeable divine knowledge. Orthodox Islamic theology holds that Allah's knowledge does not compel human actions.

Checkpoint
What is the fourth argument's conclusion about seeking refuge?
A) It is beneficial only if the act is possible.
B) It is useless whether the act is known to occur or not.
C) It changes Allah's knowledge.
D) It is a form of supplication that alters fate.

Key Takeaway
Divine foreknowledge renders the act of seeking refuge logically futile because it either cannot prevent a necessary event or is redundant for an impossible one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek refuge or make du'a, do you truly believe that Allah's knowledge is unchangeable, yet still find yourself anxious about outcomes? What would it mean for you to genuinely entrust your future to Allah's foreknowledge rather than your own efforts?
?
This argument says that seeking refuge is logically futile. How does that challenge your own practice of making du'a? Does it make you question the sincerity of your supplication, or does it push you to seek a deeper understanding of how divine knowledge and human action coexist?
?
If you accept that Allah's knowledge cannot change, what is the real purpose of your du'a? Is it to inform Allah or to transform yourself? How will this lesson change the way you approach Allah in your private moments of need?
